<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">German resistance to Nazism</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gedenkkranz_im_Bendler-Block.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="1232" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Gedenkkranz_im_Bendler-Block.jpg/220px-Gedenkkranz_im_Bendler-Block.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Gedenkkranz_im_Bendler-Block.jpg/330px-Gedenkkranz_im_Bendler-Block.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Gedenkkranz_im_Bendler-Block.jpg/440px-Gedenkkranz_im_Bendler-Block.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gedenkkranz_im_Bendler-Block.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Memorial plaque for resistance members and wreath at the <a href="/wiki/Bendlerblock" title="Bendlerblock">Bendlerblock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Memorial_to_Polish_Soldiers_and_German_Anti-Fascists_x.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2161" data-file-width="1433" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Memorial_to_Polish_Soldiers_and_German_Anti-Fascists_x.jpg/220px-Memorial_to_Polish_Soldiers_and_German_Anti-Fascists_x.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Memorial_to_Polish_Soldiers_and_German_Anti-Fascists_x.jpg/330px-Memorial_to_Polish_Soldiers_and_German_Anti-Fascists_x.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Memorial_to_Polish_Soldiers_and_German_Anti-Fascists_x.jpg/440px-Memorial_to_Polish_Soldiers_and_German_Anti-Fascists_x.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Memorial_to_Polish_Soldiers_and_German_Anti-Fascists_x.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Memorial to Polish Soldiers and German Anti-Fascists 1939-1945 in Berlin.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>German resistance to Nazism</b> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i><b>Widerstand</b></i>) was the <a href="/wiki/Anti-fascism" title="Anti-fascism">opposition</a> by individuals and groups in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">National Socialist regime</a> between 1933 and 1945. Some of these engaged in active <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assassination_attempts_on_Adolf_Hitler" title="Assassination attempts on Adolf Hitler">plans</a> to remove <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> from power and overthrow his regime.</p>
<p>The term <i>German resistance</i> should not be understood as meaning that there was a united <a href="/wiki/Resistance_movement" title="Resistance movement">resistance movement</a> in Germany at any time during the Nazi period,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klemperer.2C4-5_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-klemperer.2C4-5-1">[1]</a></sup> analogous to the more coordinated <a href="/wiki/Polish_Underground_State" title="Polish Underground State">Polish Underground State</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Resistance" title="Greek Resistance">Greek Resistance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_Partisans" title="Yugoslav Partisans">Yugoslav Partisans</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_Resistance" title="French Resistance">French Resistance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_Resistance" title="Dutch Resistance">Dutch Resistance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_resistance_movement" title="Norwegian resistance movement">Norwegian resistance movement</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Resistance" title="Italian Resistance">Italian Resistance</a>. The German resistance consisted of small and usually isolated groups. They were unable to mobilize political opposition. Save for individual attacks on Nazis (including Hitler) or sabotage acts, the only real strategy was to persuade leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a> to stage a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup</a> against the regime: the <a href="/wiki/20_July_plot" title="20 July plot">1944 assassination attempt against Hitler</a> was intended to trigger such a coup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klemperer.2C4-5_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-klemperer.2C4-5-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Approximately 77,000 German citizens were killed for one or another form of resistance by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sondergerichte" title="Sondergerichte">Special Courts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Courts-martial" title="Courts-martial">courts-martial</a>, <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Court_(Germany)" title="People's Court (Germany)">People's Court</a> and the civil justice system. Many of these Germans had served in government, the military, or in civil positions, which enabled them to engage in <a href="/wiki/Subversion" title="Subversion">subversion</a> and conspiracy; in addition the Canadian historian Peter Hoffman counts unspecified "tens of thousands" in concentration camps who were either suspected or actually engaged in opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> By contrast, the German historian <a href="/wiki/Hans_Mommsen" title="Hans Mommsen">Hans Mommsen</a> wrote that resistance in Germany was "resistance without the people" and that the number of those Germans engaged in resistance to the Nazi regime was very small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The resistance in Germany included German citizens of non-German ethnicity, such as members of the Polish minority who formed resistance groups like <a href="/wiki/Olimp_(organization)" title="Olimp (organization)">Olimp</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">Introduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Introduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The German Resistance movement consisted of several disparate political and ideological strands, which represented different classes of German society and were seldom able to work together – indeed for much of the period there was little or no contact between the different strands of resistance. A few civilian resistance groups developed, but the Army was the only organisation with the capacity to overthrow the government, and from within it a small number of officers came to present the most serious threat posed to the Nazi regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The Foreign Office and the <a href="/wiki/Abwehr" title="Abwehr">Abwehr</a> (Military Intelligence) also provided vital support to the movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> But many of those in the military who ultimately chose to seek to overthrow Hitler had initially supported the regime, if not all of its methods. Hitler's 1938 purge of the military was accompanied by increased militancy in the Nazification of Germany, a sharp intensification of the persecution of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Jews" title="German Jews">Jews</a>, homosexuals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> communists, socialists, and trade union leaders<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and daring foreign policy exploits, bringing Germany to the brink of war and it was at this time that the German Resistance emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Resistance members were motivated by such factors as the mistreatment of Jews, harassment of the churches, and the harsh actions of Himmler and the <a href="/wiki/Gestapo" title="Gestapo">Gestapo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> In his history of the German Resistance, Peter Hoffmann wrote that "National Socialism was not simply a party like any other; with its total acceptance of criminality it was an incarnation of evil, so that all those whose minds were attuned to democracy, Christianity, freedom, humanity or even mere legality found themselves forced into alliance...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>One strand was the underground networks of the banned <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democrats</a> (SPD) and <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Germany" title="Communist Party of Germany">Communists</a> (KPD), such as the SPD activist <a href="/wiki/Julius_Leber" title="Julius Leber">Julius Leber</a>, who was an active resistance figure. There was also resistance from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarcho-syndicalist" title="Anarcho-syndicalist">anarcho-syndicalist</a> union, the <a href="/wiki/Free_Workers%27_Union_of_Germany" title="Free Workers' Union of Germany">Freie Arbeiter Union</a> (FAUD) that distributed anti-Nazi propaganda and assisted people in fleeing the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-12">[12]</a></sup> Another group, the <a href="/wiki/Red_Orchestra_(espionage)" title="Red Orchestra (espionage)">Red Orchestra</a> (Rote Kapelle), consisted of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-fascist" title="Anti-fascist">anti-fascists</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communist" title="Communist">communists</a>, and an American woman. The individuals in this group began to assist their Jewish friends as early as 1933.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-074-16,_Dietrich_Bonhoeffer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="789" data-file-width="497" height="349" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-074-16%2C_Dietrich_Bonhoeffer.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-074-16%2C_Dietrich_Bonhoeffer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-074-16%2C_Dietrich_Bonhoeffer.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-074-16%2C_Dietrich_Bonhoeffer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-074-16%2C_Dietrich_Bonhoeffer.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-074-16%2C_Dietrich_Bonhoeffer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-074-16,_Dietrich_Bonhoeffer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Bonhoeffer" title="Dietrich Bonhoeffer">Dietrich Bonhoeffer</a> at Sigurdshof, 1939.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another strand was resistance coming from members of the Christian churches, Catholic and Protestant. Their stance was symbolically significant. The Churches, as institutions, did not openly advocate for the overthrow of the Nazi state, but they remained one of the very few German institutions to retain some independence from the state, and were thus able to continue to co-ordinate a level of opposition to Government policies. They resisted the regime's efforts to intrude on ecclesiastical autonomy, but from the beginning, a minority of clergymen expressed broader reservations about the new order, and gradually their criticisms came to form a "coherent, systematic critique of many of the teachings of National Socialism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theodore_S._Hamerow_p._133_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theodore_S._Hamerow_p._133-13">[13]</a></sup> Some priests - such as the Jesuits <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Delp" title="Alfred Delp">Alfred Delp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustin_R%C3%B6sch" title="Augustin Rösch">Augustin Rösch</a> and the Lutheran preacher <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Bonhoeffer" title="Dietrich Bonhoeffer">Dietrich Bonhoeffer</a> - were active and influential within the clandestine German Resistance, while figures such as Protestant Pastor <a href="/wiki/Martin_Niem%C3%B6ller" title="Martin Niemöller">Martin Niemöller</a> (who founded the <a href="/wiki/Confessing_Church" title="Confessing Church">Confessing Church</a>), and the Catholic Bishop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/August_von_Galen" title="August von Galen">August von Galen</a> (who denounced Nazi euthanasia and lawlessness), offered some of the most trenchant public criticism of the Third Reich - not only against intrusions by the regime into church governance and to arrests of clergy and expropriation of church property, but also to the fundamentals of human rights and justice as the foundation of a political system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theodore_S._Hamerow_p._288-9_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theodore_S._Hamerow_p._288-9-14">[14]</a></sup> Their example inspired some acts of overt resistance, such as that of the <a href="/wiki/White_Rose" title="White Rose">White Rose</a> student group in Munich, and provided moral stimulus and guidance for various leading figures in the political Resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> A third strand might be called the "unorganized resistance"; individual Germans or small groups of people acting in defiance of government policies or orders, or in ways seen as subversive of the Nazi system. Most notably, these included a significant number of Germans who helped <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> survive the Nazi <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocaust" title="Holocaust">Holocaust</a> by hiding them, obtaining papers for them or in other ways aiding them. More than 300 Germans have been recognised for this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It also included, particularly in the later years of the regime, informal networks of young Germans who evaded serving in the <a href="/wiki/Hitler_Youth" title="Hitler Youth">Hitler Youth</a> and defied the cultural policies of the Nazis in various ways.</p>
<p>Finally, there was the resistance network within the German Army, the Foreign Office and the <a href="/wiki/Abwehr" title="Abwehr">Abwehr</a>, the military intelligence organization. These groups hatched conspiracies against Hitler in 1938 and again in 1939, but for a variety of reasons could not implement their plans. After the German defeat in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stalingrad" title="Battle of Stalingrad">Battle of Stalingrad</a> in 1943, they contacted many army officers who were convinced that Hitler was leading Germany to disaster, although fewer who were willing to engage in overt resistance. Active resisters in this group were frequently drawn from members of the <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussian</a> aristocracy.</p>
<p>Almost every community in Germany had members taken away to concentration camps. As early as 1935 there were jingles warning: "Dear Lord God, keep me quiet, so that I don't end up in Dachau." (It almost rhymes in German: <i>Lieber Herr Gott mach mich stumm / Daß ich nicht nach Dachau komm.</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> "Dachau" refers to the <a href="/wiki/Dachau_concentration_camp" title="Dachau concentration camp">Dachau concentration camp</a>. This is a parody of a common German children's prayer, "Lieber Gott mach mich fromm, daß ich in den Himmel komm." ("Dear God, keep me pious, so I go to Heaven")</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-war_resistance_1933.E2.80.9339">Pre-war resistance 1933–39</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-war resistance 1933–39">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">

</div>
</div>
<p>There was almost no organized resistance to Hitler’s regime in the period between his appointment as chancellor in January 1933 and the crisis over <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a> in 1938. By July 1933 all other political parties and the trade unions had been suppressed, the press and radio brought under state control, and most elements of <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civil society</a> neutralised. The July 1933 <a href="/wiki/Reichskonkordat" title="Reichskonkordat">Concordat between Germany and the Holy See</a> ended any possibility of systematic resistance by the Catholic Church. The largest Protestant church, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reich_Church" title="Protestant Reich Church">German Evangelical Church</a>, was generally pro-Nazi, although a small number of church members resisted this position. The breaking of the power of the <a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">SA</a> in the "<a href="/wiki/Night_of_the_Long_Knives" title="Night of the Long Knives">Night of the Long Knives</a>" in July 1934 ended any possibility of a challenge from the "socialist" wing of the Nazi Party, and also brought the army into closer alliance with the regime.</p>
<p>All sources agree that Hitler’s regime was overwhelmingly popular with the German people during this period. The failures of the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a> had discredited democracy in the eyes of most Germans. Hitler’s apparent success in restoring full employment after the ravages of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> (achieved mainly through the reintroduction of <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a>, a policy advocating that women stay home and raise children, a crash re-armament programme, and the incremental removal of Jews from the workforce as their jobs were tendered to Gentiles), and his bloodless foreign policy successes such as the reoccupation of the <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a> in 1936 and the annexation of <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> in 1938, brought him almost universal acclaim.</p>
<p>During this period, the SPD and the KPD managed to maintain underground networks, although the legacy of pre-1933 conflicts between the two parties meant that they were unable to co-operate. The <a href="/wiki/Gestapo" title="Gestapo">Gestapo</a> frequently infiltrated these networks, and the rate of arrests and executions of SPD and KPD activists was high, but the networks continued to be able recruit new members from the industrial working class, who resented the stringent labour discipline imposed by the regime during its race to rearm. The <a href="/wiki/Sopade" title="Sopade">exiled SPD leadership</a> in <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> received and published accurate reports of events inside Germany. But beyond maintaining their existence and fomenting industrial unrest, sometimes resulting in short-lived strikes, these networks were able to achieve little.</p>
<p>There remained, however, a substantial base for opposition to Hitler’s regime. Although the Nazi Party had taken control of the German state, it had not destroyed and rebuilt the state apparatus in the way the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevik" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> regime had done in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. Institutions such as the Foreign Office, the intelligence services and, above all, the army, retained some measure of independence, while outwardly submitting to the new regime. In May 1934, Colonel-General Ludwig Beck, Chief of Staff of the Army had offered to resign if preparations were made for an offensive war against Czechoslovakia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The independence of the army was eroded in 1938, when both the War Minister, General <a href="/wiki/Werner_von_Blomberg" title="Werner von Blomberg">Werner von Blomberg</a>, and the Army Chief, General <a href="/wiki/Werner_von_Fritsch" title="Werner von Fritsch">Werner von Fritsch</a> were removed from office, but an informal network of officers critical of the Nazi regime remained.</p>
<p>In 1936, thanks to an informer, the Gestapo raids devastated <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-syndicalism" title="Anarcho-syndicalism">Anarcho-syndicalist</a> groups all over Germany, resulting in the arrest of 89 people. Most ended up either imprisoned or murdered by the regime. The groups had been encouraging strikes, printing and distributing anti-Nazi propaganda and recruiting people to fight the Nazi's fascist allies during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>As part of the agreement with the conservative forces by which Hitler became chancellor in 1933, the non-party conservative <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_von_Neurath" title="Konstantin von Neurath">Konstantin von Neurath</a> remained foreign minister, a position he retained until 1938. During Neurath's time in control, the Foreign Office with its network of diplomats and access to intelligence, became home to a circle of resistance, under the discreet patronage of the Under-Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Ernst_von_Weizs%C3%A4cker" title="Ernst von Weizsäcker">Ernst von Weizsäcker</a>. Prominent in this circle were the ambassador in <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> <a href="/wiki/Ulrich_von_Hassell" title="Ulrich von Hassell">Ulrich von Hassell</a>, the Ambassador in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Werner_von_der_Schulenburg" title="Friedrich Werner von der Schulenburg">Friedrich Graf von der Schulenburg</a>, and officials <a href="/wiki/Adam_von_Trott_zu_Solz" title="Adam von Trott zu Solz">Adam von Trott zu Solz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erich_Kordt" title="Erich Kordt">Erich Kordt</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hans-Bernd_von_Haeften" title="Hans-Bernd von Haeften">Hans-Bernd von Haeften</a>. This circle survived even when the ardent Nazi <a href="/wiki/Joachim_von_Ribbentrop" title="Joachim von Ribbentrop">Joachim von Ribbentrop</a> succeeded Neurath as foreign minister.</p>
<p>The most important centre of opposition to the regime within the state apparatus was in the intelligence services, whose clandestine operations offered an excellent cover for political organisation. The key figure here was Colonel <a href="/wiki/Hans_Oster" title="Hans Oster">Hans Oster</a>, head of the Military Intelligence Office from 1938, and an anti-Nazi from as early as 1934. He was protected by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counter-intelligence" title="Counter-intelligence">Abwehr</a> chief, Admiral <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Canaris" title="Wilhelm Canaris">Wilhelm Canaris</a>. Oster organized an extensive clandestine network of potential resisters in the army and the intelligence services. He found an early ally in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hans-Bernd_Gisevius" title="Hans-Bernd Gisevius">Hans-Bernd Gisevius</a>, a senior official in the Interior Ministry. <a href="/wiki/Hjalmar_Schacht" title="Hjalmar Schacht">Hjalmar Schacht</a>, the governor of the <a href="/wiki/Reichsbank" title="Reichsbank">Reichsbank</a>, was also in touch with this opposition.</p>
<p>The problem these groups faced, however, was what form resistance to Hitler could take in the face of the regime’s successive triumphs. They recognised that it was impossible to stage any kind of open political resistance. This was not, as is sometimes stated, because the repressive apparatus of the regime was so all-pervasive that public protest was impossible – as was shown when Catholics protested against the removal of crucifixes from Oldenburg schools in 1936, and the regime backed down. Rather it was because of Hitler’s massive support among the German people. While resistance movements in the occupied countries could mobilise patriotic sentiment against the German occupiers, in Germany the resistance risked being seen as unpatriotic, particularly in wartime. Even many army officers and officials who detested Hitler had a deep aversion to being involved in "subversive" or "treasonous" acts against the government.</p>
<p>As early as 1936 Oster and Gisevius came to the view that a regime so totally dominated by one man could only be brought down by eliminating that man – either by assassinating Hitler or by staging an army coup against him. But it was a long time before any significant number of Germans came to accept this view. Many clung to the belief that Hitler could be persuaded to moderate his regime, or that some other more moderate figure could replace him. Others argued that Hitler was not to blame for the regime’s excesses, and that the removal of <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">Heinrich Himmler</a> and reduction in the power of the <a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">SS</a> was needed. Some oppositionists were devout Christians who disapproved of assassination as a matter of principle. Others, particularly the army officers, felt bound by the <a href="/wiki/Hitler_oath" title="Hitler oath">personal oath of loyalty they had taken to Hitler</a> in 1934.</p>
<p>The opposition was also hampered by a lack of agreement about their objectives other than the need to remove Hitler from power. Some oppositionists were liberals who opposed the ideology of the Nazi regime in its entirety, and who wished to restore a system of parliamentary <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a>. Most of the army officers and many of the civil servants, however, were conservatives and nationalists, and many had initially supported Hitler’s policies – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carl_Goerdeler" title="Carl Goerdeler">Carl Goerdeler</a>, the Lord Mayor of <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a>, was a good example. Some favoured restoring the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hohenzollern_dynasty" title="Hohenzollern dynasty">Hohenzollern dynasty</a>, others favoured an authoritarian, but not Nazi, regime. Some saw no problem with Hitler's anti-Semitism and ultra-nationalism, and opposed only his apparent reckless determination to take Germany into a new world war. Because of their many differences, the opposition was unable to form a united movement, or to send a coherent message to potential allies outside Germany.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_the_churches">Role of the churches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Role of the churches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Though neither the Catholic nor Protestant churches as institutions were prepared to openly oppose the Nazi State, it was from the clergy that the first major component of the German Resistance to the policies of the Third Reich emerged, and the churches as institutions provided the earliest and most enduring centres of systematic opposition to Nazi policies. From the outset of Nazi rule in 1933, issues emerged which brought the churches into conflict with the regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_Hoffmann_p.14_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_Hoffmann_p.14-19">[19]</a></sup> They offered organised, systematic and consistent resistance to government policies which infringed on ecclesiastical autonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> As one of the few German institutions to retain some independence from the state, the churches were able to co-ordinate a level of opposition to Government, and, according to <a href="/wiki/Joachim_Fest" title="Joachim Fest">Joachim Fest</a>, they, more than any other institutions, continued to provide a "forum in which individuals could distance themselves from the regime".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plotting_Hitler.27s_Death_p.32_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plotting_Hitler.27s_Death_p.32-21">[21]</a></sup> Christian morality and the anti-Church policies of the Nazis also motivated many German resisters and provided impetus for the "moral revolt" of individuals in their efforts to overthrow Hitler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graml.2C_Mommsen_pp.198-199_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graml.2C_Mommsen_pp.198-199-22">[22]</a></sup> The historian Wolf cites events such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/July_Plot" title="July Plot">July Plot</a> of 1944 as having been "inconceivable without the spiritual support of church resistance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_Hoffmann_p.14_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_Hoffmann_p.14-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graml.2C_Mommsen_p.226_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graml.2C_Mommsen_p.226-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>"From the very beginning", wrote Hamerow, "some churchmen expressed, quite directly at times, their reservations about the new order. In fact those reservations gradually came to form a coherent, systematic critique of many of the teachings of National Socialism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theodore_S._Hamerow_p._133_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Theodore_S._Hamerow_p._133-13">[13]</a></sup> Clergy in the German Resistance had some independence from the state apparatus, and could thus criticise it, while not being close enough to the centre of power to take steps to overthrow it. "Clerical resistors", wrote Theodore S. Hamerow, could indirectly "articulate political dissent in the guise of pastoral stricture". They usually spoke out not against the established system, but "only against specific policies that it had mistakenly adopted and that it should therefore properly correct".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Later, the most trenchant public criticism of the Third Reich came from some of Germany's religious leaders, as the government was reluctant to move against them, and though they could claim to be merely attending to the spiritual welfare of their flocks, "what they had to say was at times so critical of the central doctrines of National Socialism that to say it required great boldness", and they became resistors. Their resistance was directed not only against intrusions by the government into church governance and to arrests of clergy and expropriation of church property, but also to matters like Nazi euthanasia and eugenics and to the fundamentals of human rights and justice as the foundation of a political system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theodore_S._Hamerow_p._288-9_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Theodore_S._Hamerow_p._288-9-14">[14]</a></sup> A senior cleric could rely on a degree of popular support from the faithful, and thus the regime had to consider the possibility of nationwide protests if such figures were arrested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theodore_S._Hamerow_p._133_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Theodore_S._Hamerow_p._133-13">[13]</a></sup> Thus the Catholic Bishop of Munster, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/August_von_Galen" title="August von Galen">August von Galen</a> and Dr <a href="/wiki/Theophil_Wurm" title="Theophil Wurm">Theophil Wurm</a>, the Protestant Bishop of Wurttemberg were able to rouse widespread public opposition to murder of invalids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>For figures like the Jesuit Provincial of Bavaria, <a href="/wiki/Augustin_R%C3%B6sch" title="Augustin Rösch">Augustin Rösch</a>, the Catholic trade unionists <a href="/wiki/Jakob_Kaiser" title="Jakob Kaiser">Jakob Kaiser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Letterhaus" title="Bernhard Letterhaus">Bernhard Letterhaus</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/July_Plot" title="July Plot">July Plot</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Claus_von_Stauffenberg" title="Claus von Stauffenberg">Claus von Stauffenberg</a>, "religious motives and the determination to resist would seem to have developed hand in hand".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graml.2C_Mommsen_p.225_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graml.2C_Mommsen_p.225-26">[26]</a></sup> Ernst Wolf wrote that some credit must be given to the <a href="/wiki/Kirchenkampf" title="Kirchenkampf">resistance of the churches</a>, for providing "moral stimulus and guidance for the political Resistance...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graml.2C_Mommsen_p.201_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graml.2C_Mommsen_p.201-27">[27]</a></sup> Virtually all of the military conspirators in the July Plot were religious men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graml.2C_Mommsen_p.227_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graml.2C_Mommsen_p.227-28">[28]</a></sup> Among the social democrat political conspirators, the Christian influence was also strong, though <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a> also played a significant foundational role - and among the wider circle there were other political, military and nationalist motivations at play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graml.2C_Mommsen_p.227_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graml.2C_Mommsen_p.227-28">[28]</a></sup> Religious motivations were particularly strong in the <a href="/wiki/Kreisau_Circle" title="Kreisau Circle">Kreisau Circle</a> of the Resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The Kreisau leader <a href="/wiki/Helmuth_James_Graf_von_Moltke" title="Helmuth James Graf von Moltke">Helmuth James Graf von Moltke</a> declared in one of his final letters before execution that the essence of the July revolt was "outrage of the Christian conscience".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graml.2C_Mommsen_p.226_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graml.2C_Mommsen_p.226-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the words of Kershaw, the churches "engaged in a bitter war of attrition with the regime, receiving the demonstrative backing of millions of churchgoers. Applause for Church leaders whenever they appeared in public, swollen attendances at events such as Corpus Christi Day processions, and packed church services were outward signs of the struggle of... especially of the Catholic Church - against Nazi oppression". While the Church ultimately failed to protect its youth organisations and schools, it did have some successes in mobilizing public opinion to alter government policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ian_Kershaw_pp._210-11_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ian_Kershaw_pp._210-11-30">[30]</a></sup> The churches challenged Nazi efforts to undermine various Christian institutions, practices and beliefs and Bullock wrote that "among the most courageous demonstrations of opposition during the war were the sermons preached by the <a href="/wiki/Clemens_August_Graf_von_Galen" title="Clemens August Graf von Galen">Catholic Bishop of Munster</a> and the Protestant Pastor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Neimoller" title="Martin Neimoller">Dr Niemoller</a>..." but that nevertheless, "Neither the Catholic Church nor the Evangelical Church... as institutions, felt it possible to take up an attitude of open opposition to the regime".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_resistance">Catholic resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Catholic resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Catholic_resistance_to_Nazi_Germany" title="Catholic resistance to Nazi Germany">Catholic resistance to Nazi Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pius_XII_and_the_German_Resistance" title="Pius XII and the German Resistance">Pius XII and the German Resistance</a></div>

<div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Religious_violence_in_Nigeria" title="Religious violence in Nigeria">Religious violence in Nigeria</a></div>

<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Persecutions_of_the_Catholic_Church_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>

</tr>
</table>
<p>In the 1920s and 1930s the main Christian opposition to Nazism had come from the Catholic Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> German bishops were hostile to the emerging movement and energetically denounced its "false doctrines".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> A threatening, though initially mainly sporadic persecution of the Catholic Church in Germany followed the Nazi takeover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Hitler moved quickly to eliminate <a href="/wiki/Political_Catholicism" title="Political Catholicism">Political Catholicism</a>, rounding up members of the Catholic political parties and banning their existence in July 1933. Vice Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Franz_von_Papen" title="Franz von Papen">Franz von Papen</a> meanwhile negotiated a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reich_concordat" title="Reich concordat">Reich concordat</a> with the Holy See, which prohibited clergy from participating in politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Catholic resistance initially diminished after the Concordat, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardinal_Bertram" title="Cardinal Bertram">Cardinal Bertram</a> of Breslau, the chairman of the German Conference of Bishops, developing an ineffectual protest system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plotting_Hitler.27s_Death_p.32_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plotting_Hitler.27s_Death_p.32-21">[21]</a></sup> Firmer resistance by Catholic leaders gradually reasserted itself by the individual actions of leading churchmen like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Frings" title="Joseph Frings">Joseph Frings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Konrad_von_Preysing" title="Konrad von Preysing">Konrad von Preysing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/August_von_Galen" title="August von Galen">August von Galen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_von_Faulhaber" title="Michael von Faulhaber">Michael von Faulhaber</a>. Hoffmann writes that, from the beginning:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_Hoffmann_p.14_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_Hoffmann_p.14-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"[The Catholic Church] could not silently accept the general persecution, regimentation or oppression, nor in particular the sterilization law of summer 1933. Over the years until the outbreak of war Catholic resistance stiffened until finally its most eminent spokesman was the Pope himself with his encyclial <i><a href="/wiki/Mit_brennender_Sorge" title="Mit brennender Sorge">Mit brennender Sorge</a></i>... of 14 March 1937, read from all German Catholic pulpits. Clemens August Graf von Galen, Bishop of Munster, was typical of the many fearless Catholic speakers. In general terms, therefore, the churches were the only major organisations to offer comparatively early and open resistance: they remained so in later years.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Extract from <i>The History of the German Resistance 1933-1945</i> by Peter Hoffmann</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E.Klausener0001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="629" data-file-width="516" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/E.Klausener0001.jpg/170px-E.Klausener0001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/E.Klausener0001.jpg/255px-E.Klausener0001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/E.Klausener0001.jpg/340px-E.Klausener0001.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:E.Klausener0001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Erich_Klausener" title="Erich Klausener">Erich Klausener</a>, the head of <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Action" title="Catholic Action">Catholic Action</a>, was assassinated in Hitler's bloody <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Night_of_the_long_knives" title="Night of the long knives">night of the long knives</a> purge of 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ian_Kershaw_p.315_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ian_Kershaw_p.315-37">[37]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the year following Hitler's "seizure of power", old political players looked for means to overthrow the new government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_Centre_Party" title="Catholic Centre Party">Catholic Centre Party</a> leader and Reich Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Br%C3%BCning" title="Heinrich Brüning">Heinrich Brüning</a> looked for a way to oust Hitler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_Hoffmann_p_25_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_Hoffmann_p_25-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Erich_Klausener" title="Erich Klausener">Erich Klausener</a>, an influential civil servant and president of Berlin's <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Action" title="Catholic Action">Catholic Action</a> group organised Catholic conventions in Berlin in 1933, and 1934 and spoke against political oppression to a crowd of 60,000 at the 1934 rally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Deputy Reich Chancellor von Papen, a conservative Catholic nobleman, delivered an indictment of the Nazi government in his <a href="/wiki/Marburg_speech" title="Marburg speech">Marburg speech</a> of 17 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_Hoffmann_p_25_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_Hoffmann_p_25-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> His speech writer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edgar_Jung" title="Edgar Jung">Edgar Jung</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Action" title="Catholic Action">Catholic Action</a> worker, seized the opportunity to reassert the Christian foundation of the state, pleaded for religious freedom, and rejected totalitarian aspirations in the field of religion, hoping to spur a rising, centred on Hindenberg, Papen and the army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hitler decided to strike at his chief political opponents in the <a href="/wiki/Night_of_the_Long_Knives" title="Night of the Long Knives">Night of the Long Knives</a>. The purge lasted two days over 30 June and 1 July 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Leading rivals of Hitler were killed. High-profile Catholic resistors were targeted - Klausener and Jung were murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adalbert_Probst" title="Adalbert Probst">Adalbert Probst</a>, the national director of the Catholic Youth Sports Association, was also killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis.2C_Brenda_Ralph_2000_p._45_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis.2C_Brenda_Ralph_2000_p._45-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis.2C_Brenda_Ralph_2000_p._45_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis.2C_Brenda_Ralph_2000_p._45-46">[46]</a></sup> The Catholic press was targeted too, with anti-Nazi journalist <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Gerlich" title="Fritz Gerlich">Fritz Gerlich</a> among the dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> On 2 August 1934, the aged President von Hindenberg died. The offices of President and Chancellor were combined, and Hitler ordered the Army to swear an oath directly to him. Hitler declared his "revolution" complete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cardinal Michael von Faulhaber gained an early reputation as a critic of the Nazis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_Britannica_Online_2013_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_Britannica_Online_2013-49">[49]</a></sup> His three Advent sermons of 1933, entitled <i>Judaism, Christianity, and Germany</i> denounced the Nazi extremists who were calling for the Bible to be purged of the "Jewish" Old Testament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Faulhaber tried to avoid conflict with the state over issues not strictly pertaining to the church, but on issues involving the defence of Catholics he refused to compromise or retreat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theodore_S._Hamerow_pp.200-202_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theodore_S._Hamerow_pp.200-202-51">[51]</a></sup> When in 1937 the authorities in Upper Bavaria attempted to replace Catholic schools with "common schools", he offered fierce resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theodore_S._Hamerow_pp.200-202_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Theodore_S._Hamerow_pp.200-202-51">[51]</a></sup> Among the most firm and consistent of senior Catholics to oppose the Nazis was <a href="/wiki/Konrad_von_Preysing" title="Konrad von Preysing">Konrad von Preysing</a>, Bishop of Berlin from 1935.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> He worked with leading members of the resistance <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carl_Goerdeler" title="Carl Goerdeler">Carl Goerdeler</a> an <a href="/wiki/Helmuth_James_Graf_von_Moltke" title="Helmuth James Graf von Moltke">Helmuth James Graf von Moltke</a>. He was part of the five-member commission that prepared the <i><a href="/wiki/Mit_brennender_Sorge" title="Mit brennender Sorge">Mit brennender Sorge</a></i> anti-Nazi encyclical of March 1937, and sought to block the Nazi closure of Catholic schools and arrests of church officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Hitler did not feel powerful enough to arrest senior clergy before the end of the war, an estimated one third of German priests faced some form of reprisal from the Nazi Government and 400 German priests were sent to the dedicated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Priest_Barracks_of_Dachau" title="Priest Barracks of Dachau">Priest Barracks</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dachau_Concentration_Camp" title="Dachau Concentration Camp">Dachau Concentration Camp</a> alone. Among the best known German priest martyrs were the Jesuit <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Delp" title="Alfred Delp">Alfred Delp</a> and Fr <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Lichtenberg" title="Bernhard Lichtenberg">Bernhard Lichtenberg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ian_Kershaw_pp._210-11_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ian_Kershaw_pp._210-11-30">[30]</a></sup> Lichtenberg ran Bishop von Preysing's aid unit (the <i>Hilfswerke beim Bischöflichen Ordinariat Berlin</i>) which secretly assisted those who were being persecuted by the regime. Arrested in 1941, he died en route to Dachau Concentration Camp in 1943.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anton_Gill_p.60_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anton_Gill_p.60-55">[55]</a></sup> Delp - along with fellow Jesuits <a href="/wiki/Augustin_R%C3%B6sch" title="Augustin Rösch">Augustin Rösch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lothar_K%C3%B6nig" title="Lothar König">Lothar König</a> - was among the central players of the <a href="/wiki/Kreisau_Circle" title="Kreisau Circle">Kreisau Circle</a> Resistance group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Bishop von Preysing also had contact with the group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anton_Gill_p.161_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anton_Gill_p.161-57">[57]</a></sup> The group combined conservative notions of reform with socialist strains of thought - a symbiosis expressed by Delp's notion of "personal socialism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graml.2C_Mommsen_pp_86-87_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graml.2C_Mommsen_pp_86-87-58">[58]</a></sup> Among the German laity, <a href="/wiki/Gertrud_Luckner" title="Gertrud Luckner">Gertrud Luckner</a>, was among the first to sense the genocidal inclinations of the Hitler regime and to take national action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wnlibrary.com_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-wnlibrary.com-59">[59]</a></sup> She cooperated with Lichtenberg and Delp and attempted to establish a national underground network to assist Jews through the Catholic aid agency Caritas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wnlibrary.com_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-wnlibrary.com-59">[59]</a></sup> Using international contacts she secured safe passage abroad for many refugees. She organized aid circles for Jews, assisted many to escape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Arrested in 1943, she only narrowly escaped death in the concentration camps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wnlibrary.com_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-wnlibrary.com-59">[59]</a></sup> Social worker <a href="/wiki/Margarete_Sommer" title="Margarete Sommer">Margarete Sommer</a> counselled victims of racial persecution for Caritas Emergency Relief and in 1941 became director of the Welfare Office of the Berlin Diocesan Authority, under Lichtenberg, and Bishop Preysing. She coordinated Catholic aid for victims of racial persecution - giving spiritual comfort, food, clothing, and money and wrote several reports on the mistreatment of Jews from 1942, including an August 1942 report which reached Rome under the title “Report on the Exodus of the Jews”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CAvGalenBAMS200612.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="773" data-file-width="601" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/CAvGalenBAMS200612.jpg/170px-CAvGalenBAMS200612.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/CAvGalenBAMS200612.jpg/255px-CAvGalenBAMS200612.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/CAvGalenBAMS200612.jpg/340px-CAvGalenBAMS200612.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CAvGalenBAMS200612.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Blessed <a href="/wiki/Clemens_August_Graf_von_Galen" title="Clemens August Graf von Galen">Clemens August Graf von Galen</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Munster" title="Bishop of Munster">Bishop of Munster</a>, condemned Nazi policies from the pulpit.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Even at the height of Hitler’s popularity, one issue unexpectedly provoked powerful and successful resistance to his regime. This was the programme of so-called “<a href="/wiki/Euthanasia" title="Euthanasia">euthanasia</a>” – in fact a campaign of mass murder – directed at people with mental illness and/or severe physical disabilities which had begun in 1939 under the code name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-4_Euthanasia_Program" title="T-4 Euthanasia Program">T4</a>. By 1941 more than 70,000 people had been killed under this programme, many by gassing, and their bodies incinerated. This policy aroused strong opposition across German society, and especially among Catholics. Opposition to the policy sharpened after the German attack on the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> in June 1941, because the war in the east produced for the first time large-scale German casualties, and the hospitals and asylums began to fill up with maimed and disabled young German soldiers. Rumours began to circulate that these men would also be subject to “euthanasia,” although no such plans existed.</p>
<p>Catholic anger was further fuelled by actions of the <a href="/wiki/Gauleiter" title="Gauleiter">Gauleiter</a> of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Bavaria" title="Upper Bavaria">Upper Bavaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Wagner" title="Adolf Wagner">Adolf Wagner</a>, a militantly anti-Catholic Nazi, who in June 1941 ordered the removal of crucifixes from all schools in his Gau. This attack on Catholicism provoked the first public demonstrations against government policy since the Nazis had come to power, and the mass signing of petitions, including by Catholic soldiers serving at the front. When Hitler heard of this he ordered Wagner to rescind his decree, but the damage had been done – German Catholics had learned that the regime could be successfully opposed. This led to more outspoken protests against the “euthanasia” programme.</p>
<p>In July the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bishop_of_M%C3%BCnster" title="Bishop of Münster">Bishop of Münster</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/August_von_Galen" title="August von Galen">August von Galen</a> (an old aristocratic conservative, like many of the anti-Hitler Army officers), publicly denounced the “euthanasia” programme in a sermon, and telegrammed his text to Hitler, calling on “the Führer to defend the people against the Gestapo.” Another Bishop, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franz_Bornewasser" title="Franz Bornewasser">Franz Bornewasser</a> of <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a>, also sent protests to Hitler, though not in public. On 3 August von Galen was even more outspoken, broadening his attack to include the Nazi persecution of religious orders and the closing of Catholic institutions. Local Nazis asked for Galen to be arrested, but Propaganda Minister <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goebbels" title="Joseph Goebbels">Joseph Goebbels</a> told Hitler that if this happened there would be an open revolt in Westphalia. Galen's sermons went further than defending the church, he spoke of a moral danger to Germany from the regime's violations of basic human rights: "the right to life, to inviolability, and to freedom is an indispensable part of any moral social order", he said - and any government that punishes without court proceedings "undermines its own authority and respect for its sovereignty within the conscience of its citizens".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>By August, the protests had spread to Bavaria. Hitler was jeered by an angry crowd at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hof,_Germany" title="Hof, Germany">Hof</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a> – the only time he was opposed to his face in public during his 12 years of rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Hitler knew that he could not afford a confrontation with the Church at a time when Germany was engaged in a life-and-death two-front war. (It needs to be remembered that following the annexations of <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sudetenland" title="Sudetenland">Sudetenland</a>, nearly half of all Germans were Catholic.) On 24 August he ordered the cancellation of the T4 programme and issued strict instructions to the Gauleiters that there were to be no further provocations of the churches during the war.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pius_XII" title="Pius XII">Pius XII</a> became Pope on the eve of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_War_Two" title="World War Two">World War Two</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pius_XII_and_the_German_Resistance" title="Pius XII and the German Resistance">maintained links to the German Resistance</a>. Although remaining publicly neutral, Pius advised the British in 1940 of the readiness of certain German generals to overthrow Hitler if they could be assured of an honourable peace, offered assistance to the German resistance in the event of a coup and warned the Allies of the planned German invasion of the Low Countries in 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In 1943, Pius issued the <i><a href="/wiki/Mystici_corporis_Christi" title="Mystici corporis Christi">Mystici corporis Christi</a></i> encyclical, in which he condemned the practice of killing the disabled. He stated his "profound grief" at the murder of the deformed, the insane, and those suffering from hereditary disease... as though they were a useless burden to Society", in condemnation of the ongoing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euthanasia_in_Nazi_Germany" title="Euthanasia in Nazi Germany">Nazi euthanasia program</a>. The Encyclical was followed, on 26 September 1943, by an open condemnation by the German Bishops which, from every German pulpit, denounced the killing of "innocent and defenceless mentally handicapped, incurably infirm and fatally wounded, innocent hostages, and disarmed prisoners of war and criminal offenders, people of a foreign race or descent".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, the <a href="/wiki/Deportation" title="Deportation">deportation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic" title="Second Polish Republic">Polish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priests</a> by the occupying Nazis by 1942 — after Polish resistance acts and the Dutch Catholic bishops' conference's official condemnation of anti-Semitic persecutions and deportations of Jews by the Nazis — also terrified ethnic German clergy in Germany itself, some of whom would come to share the same fate because of their resistance against the Nazi government in racial and social aspects, among them Fr. <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Lichtenberg" title="Bernhard Lichtenberg">Bernhard Lichtenberg</a>. <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">Himmler's</a> 1941 <i>Aktion Klostersturm</i> (Operation Attack-the-Monastery) had also helped to spread fear among regime-critical Catholic clergy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant_churches">Protestant churches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Protestant churches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the Nazi takeover, Hitler attempted the subjugation of the Protestant churches under a single Reich Church. He divided the Lutheran Church (Germany's main Protestant denomination) and instigated a brutal <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Jehovah%27s_Witnesses_in_Nazi_Germany" title="Persecution of Jehovah's Witnesses in Nazi Germany">persecution of Jehovah's Witnesses</a>, who refused military service and allegiance to Hitlerism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Pastor <a href="/wiki/Martin_Niem%C3%B6ller" title="Martin Niemöller">Martin Niemöller</a> responded with the <i>Pastors Emergency League</i> which re-affirmed the Bible. The movement grew into the <a href="/wiki/Confessing_Church" title="Confessing Church">Confessing Church</a>, from which some clergymen opposed the Nazi regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> By 1934, the Confessional Church had declared itself the legitimate Protestant Church of Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_L._Shirer_pp.238-239_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-William_L._Shirer_pp.238-239-72">[72]</a></sup> In response to the regime's attempt to establish a state church, in March 1935, the Confessing Church Synod announced:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_Berben_p.140_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_Berben_p.140-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We see our nation threatened with mortal danger; the danger lies in a new religion. The Church has been ordered by its Master to see that Christ is honoured by our nation in a manner befitting the Judge of the world. The Church knows that it will be called to account if the German nation turns its back on Christ without being forewarned".</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— 1935 Confessing Church Synod</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In May 1936, the Confessing Church sent Hitler a memorandum courteously objecting to the "anti-Christian" tendencies of his regime, condemning <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Semitism" title="Anti-Semitism">anti-Semitism</a> and asking for an end to interference in church affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_L._Shirer_pp.238-239_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-William_L._Shirer_pp.238-239-72">[72]</a></sup> Paul Berben wrote, "A Church envoy was sent to Hitler to protest against the religious persecutions, the concentration camps, and the activities of the Gestapo, and to demand <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a>, particularly in the press."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_Berben_p.140_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_Berben_p.140-73">[73]</a></sup> The Nazi Minister of the Interior, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Frick" title="Wilhelm Frick">Wilhelm Frick</a> responded harshly. Hundreds of pastors were arrested; Dr Weissler, a signatory to the memorandum, was killed at <a href="/wiki/Sachsenhausen_concentration_camp" title="Sachsenhausen concentration camp">Sachsenhausen concentration camp</a> and the funds of the church were confiscated and collections forbidden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_L._Shirer_pp.238-239_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-William_L._Shirer_pp.238-239-72">[72]</a></sup> Church resistance stiffened and by early 1937, Hitler had abandoned his hope of uniting the Protestant churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_Berben_p.140_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_Berben_p.140-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Confessing Church was banned on 1 July 1937. Niemöller was arrested by the <a href="/wiki/Gestapo" title="Gestapo">Gestapo</a>, and sent to the concentration camps. He remained mainly at Dachau until the fall of the regime. Theological universities were closed, and other pastors and theologians arrested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_Berben_p.140_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_Berben_p.140-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Bonhoeffer" title="Dietrich Bonhoeffer">Dietrich Bonhoeffer</a>, another leading spokesman for the Confessing Church, was from the outset a critic of the Hitler regime's racism and became active in the German Resistance – calling for Christians to speak out against Nazi atrocities. Arrested in 1943, he was implicated in the 1944 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/July_Plot" title="July Plot">July Plot</a> to assassinate Hitler and executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Resistance_in_the_Army_1938.E2.80.9342">Resistance in the Army 1938–42</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Resistance in the Army 1938–42">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Oster_Conspiracy" title="Oster Conspiracy">Oster Conspiracy</a></div>
<p>Despite the removal of Blomberg and Fritsch, the army retained considerable independence, and senior officers were able to discuss their political views in private fairly freely. In May 1938 the army leadership was made aware of Hitler’s intention of invading <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a>, even at the risk of war with <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, and/or the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. The Army Chief of Staff, General <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Beck" title="Ludwig Beck">Ludwig Beck</a>, regarded this as not only immoral but reckless, since he believed that Germany would lose such a war. Oster and Beck sent emissaries to Paris and London to advise the British and French to resist Hitler’s demands, and thereby strengthen the hand of Hitler’s opponents in the Army. Weizsäcker also sent private messages to London urging resistance. The British and French were extremely doubtful of the ability of the German opposition to overthrow the Nazi regime and ignored these messages. An official of the British Foreign Office wrote on August 28, 1938: "We have had similar visits from other emissaries of the <i>Reichsheer</i>, such as Dr. Goerdeler, but those for whom these emissaries claim to speak have never given us any reasons to suppose that they would be able or willing to take action such as would lead to the overthrow of the regime. The events of June 1934 and February 1938 do not lead one to attach much hope to energetic action by the Army against the regime"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Because of the failure of Germans to overthrow their Führer in 1938, the British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a> was convinced that the resistance comprised a group of people seemingly not well organized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Writing of the 1938 conspiracy, the German historian Klaus-Jürgen Müller observed that the conspiracy was a loosely organized collection of two different groups. One group comprising the army’s Chief of Staff General Ludwig Beck, the <i><a href="/wiki/Abwehr" title="Abwehr">Abwehr</a></i> chief, Admiral <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Canaris" title="Wilhelm Canaris">Wilhelm Canaris</a>, and the Foreign Office's State Secretary, Baron <a href="/wiki/Ernst_von_Weizs%C3%A4cker" title="Ernst von Weizsäcker">Ernst von Weizsäcker</a> were the "anti-war" group in the German government, which was determined to avoid a war in 1938 that it felt Germany would lose. This group was not committed to the overthrow of the regime but was loosely allied to another, more radical group, the "anti-Nazi" fraction centered on Colonel <a href="/wiki/Hans_Oster" title="Hans Oster">Hans Oster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bernd_Gisevius" title="Hans Bernd Gisevius">Hans Bernd Gisevius</a>, which wanted to use the crisis as an excuse for executing a <i>putsch</i> to overthrow the Nazi regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The divergent aims between these two factions produced considerable tensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The historian Eckart Conze in a 2010 interview stated about the "anti-war" group in 1938:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"An overthrow of Hitler was out of the question. The group wanted to avoid a major war and the potential catastrophic consequences for Germany. Their goal wasn't to get rid of the dictator but, as they saw it, to bring him to his senses."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In August Beck spoke openly at a meeting of army generals in Berlin about his opposition to a war with the western powers over Czechoslovakia. When Hitler was informed of this, he demanded and received Beck’s resignation. Beck was highly respected in the army and his removal shocked the officer corps. His successor as chief of staff, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Halder" title="Franz Halder">Franz Halder</a>, remained in touch with him, and was also in touch with Oster. Privately, he said that he considered Hitler “the incarnation of evil.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> During September, plans for a move against Hitler were formulated, involving General <a href="/wiki/Erwin_von_Witzleben" title="Erwin von Witzleben">Erwin von Witzleben</a>, who was the army commander of the Berlin Military Region and thus well-placed to stage a coup.</p>
<p>Oster, Gisevius, and Schacht urged Halder and Beck to stage an immediate coup against Hitler, but the army officers argued that they could only mobilize support among the officer corps for such a step if Hitler made overt moves towards war. Halder nevertheless asked Oster to draw up plans for a coup. Weizsäcker and Canaris were made aware of these plans. The conspirators disagreed on what to do about Hitler if there was a successful army coup – eventually most overcame their scruples and agreed that he must be killed so that army officers would be free from their oath of loyalty. They agreed Halder would instigate the coup when Hitler committed an overt step towards war. During the planning for the 1938 <i>putsch</i>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Goerdeler" title="Carl Friedrich Goerdeler">Carl Friedrich Goerdeler</a> was in contact through the intermediary of General <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Falkenhausen" title="Alexander von Falkenhausen">Alexander von Falkenhausen</a> with Chinese intelligence<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liang.2C_His-Huey_page_359_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liang.2C_His-Huey_page_359-81">[81]</a></sup> Most German conservatives favoured Germany’s traditional informal alliance with China, and were strongly opposed to the about-face in Germany’s Far Eastern policies effected in early 1938 by <a href="/wiki/Joachim_von_Ribbentrop" title="Joachim von Ribbentrop">Joachim von Ribbentrop</a>, who abandoned the alliance with China for an alignment with Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liang.2C_His-Huey_page_359_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Liang.2C_His-Huey_page_359-81">[81]</a></sup> As a consequence, agents of Chinese intelligence supported the proposed <i>putsch</i> as a way of restoring the Sino-German alliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liang.2C_His-Huey_page_359_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-Liang.2C_His-Huey_page_359-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Remarkably, the army commander, General <a href="/wiki/Walther_von_Brauchitsch" title="Walther von Brauchitsch">Walther von Brauchitsch</a>, was well aware of the coup preparations. He told Halder he could not condone such an act, but he did not inform Hitler, to whom he was outwardly subservient, of what he knew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> This was a striking example of the code of silent solidarity among senior German Army officers, which was to survive and provide a shield for the resistance groups down to, and in many cases beyond, the crisis of July 1944.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Munich_crisis">Munich crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Munich crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R69173,_M%C3%BCnchener_Abkommen,_Staatschefs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="548" data-file-width="800" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R69173%2C_M%C3%BCnchener_Abkommen%2C_Staatschefs.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R69173%2C_M%C3%BCnchener_Abkommen%2C_Staatschefs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R69173%2C_M%C3%BCnchener_Abkommen%2C_Staatschefs.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R69173%2C_M%C3%BCnchener_Abkommen%2C_Staatschefs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R69173%2C_M%C3%BCnchener_Abkommen%2C_Staatschefs.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R69173%2C_M%C3%BCnchener_Abkommen%2C_Staatschefs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R69173,_M%C3%BCnchener_Abkommen,_Staatschefs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
From left to right, <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Daladier" title="Édouard Daladier">Édouard Daladier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> and Italian Foreign Minister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Count_Ciano" title="Count Ciano">Count Ciano</a> as they prepare to sign the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">Munich Agreement</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 13 September, the British Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a>, announced that he would visit Germany to meet Hitler and defuse the crisis over Czechoslovakia. This threw the conspirators into uncertainty. When, on 20 September, it appeared that the negotiations had broken down and that Chamberlain would resist Hitler’s demands, the coup preparations were revived and finalised. All that was required was the signal from Halder.</p>
<p>On 28 September, however, Chamberlain backed down and agreed to a <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">meeting in Munich</a>, at which he accepted the dismemberment of Czechoslovakia. This plunged the resistance into demoralisation and division. Halder said he would no longer support a coup. The other conspirators were bitterly critical of Chamberlain, but were powerless to act. This was the nearest approach to a successful conspiracy against Hitler before the plot of 20 July 1944.</p>
<p>As war again grew more likely in mid-1939, the plans for a pre-emptive coup were revived. Oster was still in contact with Halder and Witzleben, although Witzleben had been transferred to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankfurt_am_Main" title="Frankfurt am Main">Frankfurt am Main</a>, reducing his ability to lead a coup attempt. At a meeting with Goerdeler, Witzleben agreed to form a network of army commanders willing to take part to prevent a war against the western powers. But support in the officer corps for a coup had dropped sharply since 1938. Most officers, particularly those from Prussian landowning backgrounds, were strongly anti-Polish. Just before the invasion of <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> in August 1939, General <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Wagner" title="Eduard Wagner">Eduard Wagner</a> who was one of the officers involved in the abortive <i>putsch</i> of September 1938, wrote in a letter to his wife: “We believe we will make quick work of the Poles, and in truth, we are delighted at the prospect. That business <i>must</i> be cleared up" (Emphasis in the original)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillgruber.2C_Andreas_page_77_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hillgruber.2C_Andreas_page_77-83">[83]</a></sup> The German historian <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Hillgruber" title="Andreas Hillgruber">Andreas Hillgruber</a> commented that in 1939 the rampant <a href="/wiki/Anti-Polish_sentiment" title="Anti-Polish sentiment">anti-Polish</a> feelings in the German Army officer corps served to bind the military together with Hitler in supporting <a href="/wiki/Fall_Weiss_(1939)" title="Fall Weiss (1939)">Fall Weiss</a> in a way that <a href="/wiki/Fall_Gr%C3%BCn_(Czechoslovakia)" title="Fall Grün (Czechoslovakia)">Fall Grün</a> did not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillgruber.2C_Andreas_page_77_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hillgruber.2C_Andreas_page_77-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>This nevertheless marked an important turning point. In 1938 the plan had been for the army, led by Halder and if possible Brauchitsch, to depose Hitler. This was now impossible, and a conspiratorial organisation was to be formed in the army and civil service instead.</p>
<p>The opposition again urged Britain and France to stand up to Hitler: Halder met secretly with the British Ambassador Sir <a href="/wiki/Nevile_Henderson" title="Nevile Henderson">Nevile Henderson</a> to urge resistance. The plan was again to stage a coup at the moment Hitler moved to declare war. But although Britain and France were now prepared to go to war over Poland, as war approached Halder lost his nerve. Schacht, Gisevius and Canaris developed a plan to confront Brauchitsch and Halder and demand that they depose Hitler and prevent war, but nothing came of this. When Hitler invaded Poland on 1 September, the conspirators were unable to act.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outbreak_of_war">Outbreak of war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Outbreak of war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The outbreak of war made the further mobilization of resistance in the army more difficult. Halder continued to vacillate. In late 1939 and early 1940 he opposed Hitler’s plans to attack France, and kept in touch with the opposition through General <a href="/wiki/Carl-Heinrich_von_St%C3%BClpnagel" title="Carl-Heinrich von Stülpnagel">Carl-Heinrich von Stülpnagel</a>, an active oppositionist. Talk of a coup again began to circulate, and for the first time the idea of killing Hitler with a bomb was taken up by the more determined members of the resistance circles, such as Oster and Erich Kordt, who declared himself willing to do the deed. At the army headquarters at <a href="/wiki/Zossen" title="Zossen">Zossen</a>, south of Berlin, a group of officers called Action Group Zossen was also planning a coup.</p>
<p>When in November 1939 it seemed that Hitler was about to order an immediate attack in the west, the conspirators persuaded General <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Ritter_von_Leeb" title="Wilhelm Ritter von Leeb">Wilhelm Ritter von Leeb</a>, commander of Army Group C on the Belgian border, to support a planned coup if Hitler gave such an order. At the same time Oster warned the Dutch and the Belgians that Hitler was about to attack them – his warnings were not believed.
