But when Hitler postponed the attack until 1940, the conspiracy again lost momentum, and Halder formed the view that the German people would not accept a coup. Again, the chance was lost.</p>
<p><br/></p>
<p>With Poland overrun but France and the Low Countries yet to be attacked, the German Resistance sought the Pope's assistance in preparations for a coup to oust Hitler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Toland_p.760_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Toland_p.760-85">[85]</a></sup> In the winter of 1939/40, the Bavarian lawyer and reserve 'Abwehr' officer <a href="/wiki/Josef_M%C3%BCller_(CSU_politician)" title="Josef Müller (CSU politician)">Josef Müller</a>, acting as an emissary for the military opposition centered around General Franz Halder, contacted Monsignore <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Kaas" title="Ludwig Kaas">Ludwig Kaas</a>, the exiled leader of the German Catholic <a href="/wiki/Centre_Party_(Germany)" title="Centre Party (Germany)">Zentrum</a> party, in Rome, hoping to use the Pope as an intermediary to contact the British.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Kaas put Müller in contact with Father <a href="/wiki/Robert_Leiber" title="Robert Leiber">Robert Leiber</a>, who personally asked the Pope to relay the information about the German resistance to the British.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-c87_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-c87-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Vatican considered Müller to be a representative of Colonel-General von Beck and agreed to offer the machinery for mediation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_Hoffmann_p.160_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_Hoffmann_p.160-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_L._Shirer_p648-9_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-William_L._Shirer_p648-9-89">[89]</a></sup> Oster, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Canaris" title="Wilhelm Canaris">Wilhelm Canaris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hans_von_Dohn%C3%A1nyi" title="Hans von Dohnányi">Hans von Dohnányi</a>, backed by Beck, told Müller to ask Pius to ascertain whether the British would enter negotiations with the German opposition which wanted to overthrow Hitler. The British agreed to negotiate, provided the Vatican could vouch for the opposition's representative. Pius, communicating with Britain's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francis_d%27Arcy_Osborne" title="Francis d'Arcy Osborne">Francis d'Arcy Osborne</a>, channelled communications back and forth in secrecy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_Hoffmann_p.160_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_Hoffmann_p.160-88">[88]</a></sup> The Vatican agreed to send a letter outlining the bases for peace with England and the participation of the Pope was used to try to persuade senior German Generals Halder and Brauchitsch to act against Hitler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Toland_p.760_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-John_Toland_p.760-85">[85]</a></sup> Negotiations were tense, with a Western offensive expected, and on the basis that substantive negotiations could only follow the replacement of the Hitler regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_Hoffmann_p.160_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_Hoffmann_p.160-88">[88]</a></sup> Pius, without offering endorsement, advised Osbourne on 11 January 1940 that the German opposition had said that a German offensive was planned for February, but that this could be averted if the German generals could be assured of peace with Britain, and not on punitive terms. If this could be assured, then they were willing to move to replace Hitler. The British government had doubts as to the capacity of the conspirators. On 7 February, the Pope updated Osbourne that the opposition wanted to replace the Nazi regime with a democratic federation, but hoped to retain Austria and the Sudetenland. The British government was non-committal, and said that while the federal model was of interest, the promises and sources of the opposition were too vague. Nevertheless, the resistance were encouraged by the talks, and Muller told his contact that a coup would occur in February. Pius appeared to continue to hope for a coup in Germany into March 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the Fall of France, peace overtures continued to emanate from the Vatican as well as Sweden and the United States, to which Churchill responded resolutely that Germany would first have to free its conquered territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The negotiations ultimately proved fruitless. Hitler's swift victories over France and the Low Countries deflated the will of the German military to resist Hitler. Muller was arrested during the Nazis first raid on Military Intelligence in 1943. He spent the rest of the war in concentration camps, ending up at Dachau.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>The failed plots of 1938 and 1939 showed both the strength and weakness of the officer corps as potential leaders of a resistance movement. Its strength was its loyalty and solidarity. As <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Istvan_Deak" title="Istvan Deak">Istvan Deak</a> noted: “Officers, especially of the highest ranks, had been discussing, some as early as 1934… the possibility of deposing or even assassinating Hitler. Yet it seems that not a single one was betrayed by a comrade-in-arms to the Gestapo.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Remarkably, in over two years of plotting, this widespread and loosely structured conspiracy was never detected. One explanation is that at this time Himmler was still preoccupied with the traditional enemies of the Nazis, the SPD and the KPD (and, of course, the Jews), and did not suspect that the real centre of opposition was within the state itself. Another factor was Canaris’ success in shielding the plotters, particularly Oster, from suspicion.</p>
<p>The corresponding weakness of the officer corps was its conception of loyalty to the state and its aversion to mutiny. This explains the vacillations of Halder, who could never quite bring himself to take the decisive step. Halder hated Hitler, and believed that the Nazis were leading Germany to catastrophe. He was shocked and disgusted by the behaviour of the SS in occupied Poland, but gave no support to his senior officer there, General <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Blaskowitz" title="Johannes Blaskowitz">Johannes Blaskowitz</a>, when the latter officially protested to Hitler about the atrocities against the Poles and the Jews. In 1938 and again in 1939, he lost his nerve and could not give the order to strike against Hitler. This was even more true of Brauchitsch, who knew of the conspiracies and assured Halder that he agreed with their objectives, but would not take any action to support them.</p>
<p>The outbreak of war served to rally the German people around the Hitler regime, and the sweeping early successes of the German Army – occupying Poland in 1939, Denmark and Norway in April 1940, and swiftly defeating France in May and June 1940, stilled virtually all opposition to the regime. The opposition to Hitler within the Army was left isolated and apparently discredited, since the much-feared war with the western powers had apparently been won by Germany within a year and at little cost. This mood continued well into 1941, although beneath the surface popular discontent at mounting economic hardship was apparent.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="First_assassination_attempt">First assassination attempt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: First assassination attempt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E12329,_M%C3%BCnchen,_B%C3%BCrgerbr%C3%A4ukeller,_Sprengstoffanschlag.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="800" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E12329%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_B%C3%BCrgerbr%C3%A4ukeller%2C_Sprengstoffanschlag.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E12329%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_B%C3%BCrgerbr%C3%A4ukeller%2C_Sprengstoffanschlag.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E12329%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_B%C3%BCrgerbr%C3%A4ukeller%2C_Sprengstoffanschlag.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E12329%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_B%C3%BCrgerbr%C3%A4ukeller%2C_Sprengstoffanschlag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E12329%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_B%C3%BCrgerbr%C3%A4ukeller%2C_Sprengstoffanschlag.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E12329%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_B%C3%BCrgerbr%C3%A4ukeller%2C_Sprengstoffanschlag.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E12329,_M%C3%BCnchen,_B%C3%BCrgerbr%C3%A4ukeller,_Sprengstoffanschlag.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ruins of the <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BCrgerbr%C3%A4ukeller" title="Bürgerbräukeller">Bürgerbräukeller</a> in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georg_Elser" title="Georg Elser">Georg Elser</a>'s failed assassination of Hitler in November 1939</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first resolute attempt to remove Hitler during this period was led by Oster from the Abwehr, but no shot was fired. In November 1939, however, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georg_Elser" title="Georg Elser">Georg Elser</a>, a carpenter from <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Württemberg">Württemberg</a>, acting completely on his own, developed a plan to assassinate Hitler. Elser had been peripherally involved with the KPD before 1933, but his exact motives for acting as he did remain a mystery. He read in the newspapers that Hitler would be addressing a Nazi Party meeting on 8 November, in the <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BCrgerbr%C3%A4ukeller" title="Bürgerbräukeller">Bürgerbräukeller</a>, a beer hall in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> where Hitler had launched the <a href="/wiki/Beer_Hall_Putsch" title="Beer Hall Putsch">Beer Hall Putsch</a> on the same date in 1923. Stealing explosives from his workplace, Elser built a powerful time-bomb. For over a month, he managed to stay inside the Bürgerbräukeller after closing hours each night, during which time he hollowed out the pillar behind the speaker's rostrum to place the bomb inside.</p>
<p>On the night of 7 November 1939, Elser set the timer and left for the Swiss border. Unexpectedly, because of the pressure of wartime business, Hitler made a much shorter speech than usual and left the hall 13 minutes before the bomb went off, killing seven people. Sixty-three people were injured, sixteen more were seriously injured with one dying later. Had Hitler still been speaking, the bomb almost certainly would have killed him, with consequences which can only be guessed. Elser was arrested at the border, sent to the Sachsenhausen Concentration Camp, and then in 1945 moved to the <a href="/wiki/Dachau_concentration_camp" title="Dachau concentration camp">Dachau concentration camp</a>. Elser was executed two weeks before the liberation of Dachau KZ. This attempt on Hitler’s life set off a witch-hunt for potential conspirators which intimidated the opposition and made further action more difficult.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nadir_of_resistance:_1940.E2.80.9342">Nadir of resistance: 1940–42</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nadir of resistance: 1940–42">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(March 2008)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The sweeping success of Hitler’s attack on France in May 1940 made the task of deposing him even more difficult. Most army officers, their fears of a war against the western powers apparently proven groundless, and gratified by Germany’s revenge against France for the defeat of 1918, reconciled themselves to Hitler’s regime, choosing to ignore its darker side. The task of leading the resistance groups for a time fell to civilians, although a hard core of military plotters remained active.</p>
<p>Carl Goerdeler, the former lord mayor of Leipzig, emerged as a key figure. His associates included the diplomat <a href="/wiki/Ulrich_von_Hassell" title="Ulrich von Hassell">Ulrich von Hassell</a>, the Prussian Finance Minister <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Popitz" title="Johannes Popitz">Johannes Popitz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Helmuth_James_Graf_von_Moltke" title="Helmuth James Graf von Moltke">Helmuth James Graf von Moltke</a>, heir to a famous name and the leading figure in the <a href="/wiki/Kreisau_Circle" title="Kreisau Circle">Kreisau Circle</a> of Prussian oppositionists, which included other young aristocrats such as <a href="/wiki/Adam_von_Trott_zu_Solz" title="Adam von Trott zu Solz">Adam von Trott zu Solz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Yorck_von_Wartenburg" title="Peter Yorck von Wartenburg">Peter Yorck von Wartenburg</a>, and later <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Graf_von_Bismarck-Sch%C3%B6nhausen" title="Gottfried Graf von Bismarck-Schönhausen">Gottfried Graf von Bismarck-Schönhausen</a>, who was a Nazi member of the Reichstag and a senior officer in the SS. Goerdeler was also in touch with the SPD underground, whose most prominent figure was <a href="/wiki/Julius_Leber" title="Julius Leber">Julius Leber</a>, and with Christian opposition groups, both Catholic and Protestant.</p>
<p>These men saw themselves as the leaders of a post-Hitler government, but they had no clear conception of how to bring this about, except through assassinating Hitler – a step which many of them still opposed on ethical grounds. Their plans could never surmount the fundamental problem of Hitler’s overwhelming popularity among the German people. They preoccupied themselves with philosophical debates and devising grand schemes for postwar Germany. The fact was that for nearly two years after the defeat of France, there was little scope for opposition activity.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-53,_Henning_v._Tresckow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="707" data-file-width="501" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-53%2C_Henning_v._Tresckow.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-53%2C_Henning_v._Tresckow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-53%2C_Henning_v._Tresckow.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-53%2C_Henning_v._Tresckow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-53%2C_Henning_v._Tresckow.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-53%2C_Henning_v._Tresckow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-53,_Henning_v._Tresckow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Henning_von_Tresckow" title="Henning von Tresckow">Henning von Tresckow</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In March 1941 Hitler revealed his plans for a “war of annihilation” against the Soviet Union to selected army officers in a speech given in <a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84" title="Poznań">Posen</a>. In the audience was Colonel <a href="/wiki/Henning_von_Tresckow" title="Henning von Tresckow">Henning von Tresckow</a>, who had not been involved in any of the earlier plots but was already a firm opponent of the Nazi regime. He was horrified by Hitler’s plan to unleash a new and even more terrible war in the east. As a nephew of Field Marshal <a href="/wiki/Fedor_von_Bock" title="Fedor von Bock">Fedor von Bock</a>, he was very well connected. Assigned to the staff of his uncle’s command, Army Group Centre, for the forthcoming <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Operation Barbarossa</a>, Tresckow systematically recruited oppositionists to the group’s staff, making it the new nerve centre of the army resistance.</p>
<p>American journalist <a href="/wiki/Howard_K._Smith" title="Howard K. Smith">Howard K. Smith</a> wrote in 1942 that of the three groups in opposition to Hitler, the military was more important than the churches and the Communists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith1942_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith1942-94">[94]</a></sup> Little could be done while Hitler’s armies advanced triumphantly into the western regions of the Soviet Union through 1941 and 1942 – even after the setback before <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> in December 1941 that led to the dismissal of both Brauchitsch and Bock. In December 1941 the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> entered the war, persuading some more realistic army officers that Germany must ultimately lose the war. But the life-and-death struggle on the eastern front posed new problems for the resistance. Most of its members were conservatives who hated and feared <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a> and the Soviet Union. How could the Nazi regime be overthrown and the war ended without allowing the Soviets to gain control of Germany or the whole of Europe? This question was made more acute when the Allies adopted their policy of demanding Germany’s “unconditional surrender” at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Casablanca_Conference_(1943)" title="Casablanca Conference (1943)">Casablanca Conference</a> of January 1943.</p>
<p>During 1942 the tireless Oster nevertheless succeeded in rebuilding an effective resistance network. His most important recruit was General <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Olbricht" title="Friedrich Olbricht">Friedrich Olbricht</a>, head of the General Army Office headquartered at the <a href="/wiki/Bendlerblock" title="Bendlerblock">Bendlerblock</a> in central Berlin, who controlled an independent system of communications to reserve units all over Germany. Linking this asset to Tresckow’s resistance group in Army Group Centre created what appeared to a viable structure for a new effort at organising a coup. Bock’s dismissal did not weaken Tresckow’s position. In fact he soon enticed Bock’s successor, General <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnther_von_Kluge" title="Günther von Kluge">Hans von Kluge</a>, at least part-way to supporting the resistance cause. Tresckow even brought Goerdeler, leader of the civilian resistance, to Army Group Centre to meet Kluge – an extremely dangerous tactic.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Communist_resistance">Communist resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Communist resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(March 2008)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Memorial_to_Harro_Schulze-Boysen.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Memorial_to_Harro_Schulze-Boysen.JPG/220px-Memorial_to_Harro_Schulze-Boysen.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Memorial_to_Harro_Schulze-Boysen.JPG/330px-Memorial_to_Harro_Schulze-Boysen.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Memorial_to_Harro_Schulze-Boysen.JPG/440px-Memorial_to_Harro_Schulze-Boysen.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Memorial_to_Harro_Schulze-Boysen.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Memorial to Harro Schulze-Boysen, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niederkirchnerstrasse" title="Niederkirchnerstrasse">Niederkirchnerstrasse</a>, Berlin</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The entry of the Soviet Union into the war had certain consequences for the civilian resistance. During the period of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi-Soviet_Pact" title="Nazi-Soviet Pact">Nazi-Soviet Pact</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Germany" title="Communist Party of Germany">KPD</a>’s only objective inside Germany was to keep itself in existence: it engaged in no active resistance to the Nazi regime. After June 1941, however, all Communists were expected to throw themselves into resistance work, including sabotage and espionage where this was possible, regardless of risk. A handful of Soviet agents, mostly exiled German Communists, were able to enter Germany to help the scattered underground KPD cells organise and take action. This led to the formation in 1942 of two separate communist groups, usually erroneously lumped together under the name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Orchestra_(spy)" title="Red Orchestra (spy)">Rote Kapelle</a> (“Red Orchestra”), a codename given to these groups by the Gestapo.</p>
<p>The first “Red Orchestra” was an espionage network based in Berlin and coordinated by <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Trepper" title="Leopold Trepper">Leopold Trepper</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GRU" title="GRU">GRU</a> agent sent into Germany in October 1941. This group made reports to the Soviet Union on German troop concentrations, air attacks on Germany, German aircraft production, and German fuel shipments. In France, it worked with the underground <a href="/wiki/French_Communist_Party" title="French Communist Party">French Communist Party</a>. Agents of this group even managed to tap the phone lines of the Abwehr in Paris. Trepper was eventually arrested and the group broken up by the spring of 1943.</p>
<p>The second and more important “Red Orchestra” group was entirely separate and was a genuine German resistance group, not controlled by the <a href="/wiki/NKVD" title="NKVD">NKVD</a> (the Soviet intelligence agency and predecessor to the <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a>). This group was led by <a href="/wiki/Harro_Schulze-Boysen" title="Harro Schulze-Boysen">Harro Schulze-Boysen</a>, an intelligence officer at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reich_Air_Ministry" title="Reich Air Ministry">Reich Air Ministry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arvid_Harnack" title="Arvid Harnack">Arvid Harnack</a>, an official in the Ministry of Economics, both self-identified communists but not apparently KPD members. The group however contained people of various beliefs and affiliations. It included the theatre producer <a href="/wiki/Adam_Kuckhoff" title="Adam Kuckhoff">Adam Kuckhoff</a>, the author <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnther_Weisenborn" title="Günther Weisenborn">Günther Weisenborn</a>, the journalist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=John_Graudenz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="John Graudenz (page does not exist)">John Graudenz</a> and the pianist <a href="/wiki/Helmut_Roloff" title="Helmut Roloff">Helmut Roloff</a>. It thus conformed to the general pattern of German resistance groups of being drawn mainly from elite groups.</p>
<p>The main activity of the group was collecting information about Nazi atrocities and distributing leaflets against Hitler rather than espionage. They passed what they had learned to foreign countries, through personal contacts with the U.S. embassy and, via a less direct connection, to the Soviet government. When Soviet agents tried to enlist this group in their service, Schulze-Boysen and Harnack refused, since they wanted to maintain their political independence. The group was betrayed to the Gestapo in August 1942 by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Johann_Wenzel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Johann Wenzel (page does not exist)">Johann Wenzel</a>, a member of the Trepper group who also knew of the Schulze-Boysen group and who informed on them after being arrested. Schulze-Boysen, Harnack and other members of the group were arrested and secretly executed.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, another Communist resistance group was operating in Berlin, led by a Jewish electrician, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Baum" title="Herbert Baum">Herbert Baum</a>, and involving up to a hundred people. Until 1941 the group operated a study circle, but after the German attack on the Soviet Union a core group advanced to active resistance. In May 1942, the group staged an arson attack on an anti-Soviet propaganda display at the <a href="/wiki/Lustgarten" title="Lustgarten">Lustgarten</a> in central Berlin. The attack was poorly organised and most of the Baum group was arrested. Twenty were sentenced to death, while Baum himself "died in custody." This fiasco ended overt Communist resistance activities, although the KPD underground continued to operate, and re-emerged from hiding in the last days of the war.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aeroplane_assassination_attempt">Aeroplane assassination attempt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Aeroplane assassination attempt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In late 1942 von Tresckow and Olbricht formulated a plan to assassinate Hitler and stage a coup. On 13 March 1943, returning from his easternmost headquarters FHQ <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wehrwolf" title="Wehrwolf">Wehrwolf</a> near <a href="/wiki/Vinnytsia" title="Vinnytsia">Vinnitsa</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wolfsschanze" title="Wolfsschanze">Wolfsschanze</a> in East Prussia, Hitler was scheduled to make a stop-over at the headquarters of <a href="/wiki/Army_Group_Centre" title="Army Group Centre">Army Group Centre</a> at <a href="/wiki/Smolensk" title="Smolensk">Smolensk</a>. For such an occasion, von Tresckow had prepared three options:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>Major <a href="/wiki/Georg_von_Boeselager" title="Georg von Boeselager">Georg von Boeselager</a>, in command of a cavalry honor guard, could intercept Hitler in a forest and overwhelm the SS bodyguard and the Führer in a fair fight; this course was rejected because of the prospect of a large number of German soldiers fighting each other, and a possible failure regarding the unexpected strength of the escort.</li>
<li>A joint assassination could be carried out during dinner; this idea was abandoned as supporting officers abhorred the idea of shooting the unarmed tyrant.</li>
<li>A bomb could be smuggled on Hitler's plane.</li>
</ol>
<p>Von Tresckow asked Lieutenant Colonel <a href="/wiki/Heinz_Brandt" title="Heinz Brandt">Heinz Brandt</a>, on Hitler's staff and usually on the same plane that carried Hitler, to take a parcel with him, supposedly the prize of a bet won by Tresckow's friend <a href="/wiki/Helmuth_Stieff" title="Helmuth Stieff">General Stieff</a>. It concealed a bomb, disguised in a box for two bottles of <a href="/wiki/Cointreau" title="Cointreau">Cointreau</a>. Von Tresckow's aide, Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Fabian_von_Schlabrendorff" title="Fabian von Schlabrendorff">Fabian von Schlabrendorff</a>, set the fuse and handed over the parcel to Brandt who boarded the same plane as Hitler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hitler’s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Focke-Wulf_Fw_200" title="Focke-Wulf Fw 200">Focke-Wulf Fw 200</a> <i>Condor</i> was expected to explode about 30 minutes later near <a href="/wiki/Minsk" title="Minsk">Minsk</a>, close enough to the front to be attributed to Soviet fighters. Olbricht was to use the resulting crisis to mobilise his Reserve Army network to seize power in Berlin, Vienna, Munich and in the German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wehrkreis" title="Wehrkreis">Wehrkreis</a> centres. It was an ambitious but credible plan, and might have worked if Hitler had indeed been killed, although persuading Army units to fight and overcome what could certainly have been fierce resistance from the SS could have been a major obstacle.</p>
<p>But, as with Elser’s bomb in 1939 and all other attempts, luck favoured Hitler again, which was attributed to "Vorsehung" (<a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">providence</a>). The British-made chemical <a href="/wiki/Pencil_detonator" title="Pencil detonator">pencil detonator</a> on the bomb had been tested many times and was considered reliable. It went off, but the bomb did not. The <a href="/wiki/Percussion_cap" title="Percussion cap">percussion cap</a> apparently became too cold as the parcel was carried in the unheated cargo hold.</p>
<p>Displaying great <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sangfroid" title="wiktionary:sangfroid">sangfroid</a></i>, Schlabrendorff took the next plane to retrieve the package from Colonel Brandt before the content was discovered. The blocks of plastic explosives were later used by Gersdorff and Stauffenberg.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Suicide_bombing_attempts">Suicide bombing attempts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Suicide bombing attempts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-51,_Rudolf-Christoph_v._Gersdorff.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="555" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-51%2C_Rudolf-Christoph_v._Gersdorff.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-51%2C_Rudolf-Christoph_v._Gersdorff.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-51%2C_Rudolf-Christoph_v._Gersdorff.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-51%2C_Rudolf-Christoph_v._Gersdorff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-51%2C_Rudolf-Christoph_v._Gersdorff.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-51%2C_Rudolf-Christoph_v._Gersdorff.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-130-51,_Rudolf-Christoph_v._Gersdorff.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Christoph_Freiherr_von_Gersdorff" title="Rudolf Christoph Freiherr von Gersdorff">Rudolf Christoph Freiherr von Gersdorff</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A second attempt was made a few days later on 21 March 1943, when Hitler visited an exhibition of captured Soviet weaponry in Berlin's <a href="/wiki/Zeughaus" title="Zeughaus">Zeughaus</a>. One of Tresckow’s friends, Colonel <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Christoph_Freiherr_von_Gersdorff" title="Rudolf Christoph Freiherr von Gersdorff">Rudolf Christoph Freiherr von Gersdorff</a>, was scheduled to explain some exhibits, and volunteered to carry out a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suicide_bombing" title="Suicide bombing">suicide bombing</a> using the same bomb that had failed to go off on the plane, concealed on his person. But the only new chemical fuse he could obtain was a ten-minute one. Hitler again left prematurely after hurrying through the exhibition much quicker than the scheduled 30 minutes. Gersdorff had to dash to a bathroom to defuse the bomb to save his life, and more importantly, prevent any suspicion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> This second failure temporarily demoralised the plotters at Army Group Centre. Gersdorff reported about the attempt after the war; the footage is often seen on German TV documentaries ("Die Nacht des Widerstands" etc.), including a photo showing Gersdorff and Hitler.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axel_von_dem_Bussche" title="Axel von dem Bussche">Axel von dem Bussche</a>, member of the elite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infantry_Regiment_9" title="Infantry Regiment 9">Infantry Regiment 9</a>, volunteered to kill Hitler with hand grenades in November 1943 during a presentation of new winter uniforms, but the train containing them was destroyed by Allied bombs in Berlin, and the event had to be postponed. A second presentation scheduled for December at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wolfsschanze" title="Wolfsschanze">Wolfsschanze</a> was canceled on short notice as Hitler decided to travel to Berchtesgaden.</p>
<p>In January 1944, Bussche volunteered for another assassination attempt, but then he lost a leg in Russia. On February 11 another young officer, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ewald_Heinrich_von_Kleist" title="Ewald Heinrich von Kleist">Ewald Heinrich von Kleist</a> tried to assassinate Hitler in the same way von dem Bussche had planned. However Hitler again canceled the event which would have allowed Kleist to approach him.</p>
<p>On 11 March 1944 <a href="/wiki/Eberhard_von_Breitenbuch" title="Eberhard von Breitenbuch">Eberhard von Breitenbuch</a> volunteered for an assassination attempt at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berghof_(Hitler)" title="Berghof (Hitler)">Berghof</a> using a 7.65 mm Browning pistol concealed in his trouser pocket. He was not able to carry out the plan because guards would not allow him into the conference room with the <a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrer" title="Führer">Führer</a>.</p>
<p>The next occasion was a weapons exhibition on July 7 at <a href="/wiki/Schloss_Klessheim" title="Schloss Klessheim">Schloss Klessheim</a> near Salzburg, but <a href="/wiki/Helmuth_Stieff" title="Helmuth Stieff">Helmuth Stieff</a> did not trigger the bomb.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="After_Stalingrad">After Stalingrad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: After Stalingrad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011,_Russland,_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="517" data-file-width="800" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011%2C_Russland%2C_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011%2C_Russland%2C_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011%2C_Russland%2C_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011%2C_Russland%2C_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011%2C_Russland%2C_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011%2C_Russland%2C_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011,_Russland,_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Red Army soldier marches a German soldier into captivity after the victory at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stalingrad" title="Battle of Stalingrad">Battle of Stalingrad</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the end of 1942 Germany suffered a series of military defeats, the first at <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_El_Alamein" title="Second Battle of El Alamein">El Alamein</a>, the second with the successful Allied landings in North Africa (<a href="/wiki/Operation_Torch" title="Operation Torch">Operation Torch</a>), and the third the disastrous defeat at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stalingrad" title="Battle of Stalingrad">Stalingrad</a>, which ended any hope of defeating the Soviet Union. Most experienced senior officers now came to the conclusion that Hitler was leading Germany to defeat, and that the result of this would be the Soviet conquest of Germany – the worst fate imaginable. This gave the military resistance new impetus.</p>
<p>Halder had been dismissed in 1942 and there was now no independent central leadership of the Army. His nominal successors, Field Marshal <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Keitel" title="Wilhelm Keitel">Wilhelm Keitel</a> and General <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Jodl" title="Alfred Jodl">Alfred Jodl</a>, were no more than Hitler’s messengers. Tresckow and Goerdeler tried again to recruit the senior Army field commanders to support a seizure of power. Kluge was by now won over completely. Gersdorff was sent to see Field Marshal <a href="/wiki/Erich_von_Manstein" title="Erich von Manstein">Erich von Manstein</a>, the commander of Army Group South in the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>. Manstein agreed that Hitler was leading Germany to defeat, but told Gersdorff that “Prussian field marshals do not mutiny.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Field Marshal <a href="/wiki/Gerd_von_Rundstedt" title="Gerd von Rundstedt">Gerd von Rundstedt</a>, commander in the west, gave a similar answer. The prospect of a united German Army seizing power from Hitler was as far away as ever. Once again, however, neither officer reported that they had been approached in this way.</p>
<p>Nevertheless, the days when the military and civilian plotters could expect to escape detection were ending. After Stalingrad, Himmler would have had to be naïve not to expect that conspiracies against the regime would be hatched in the Army and elsewhere. He already suspected Canaris and his subordinates at the Abwehr. In March 1943 two of them, Oster and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hans_von_Dohnanyi" title="Hans von Dohnanyi">Hans von Dohnanyi</a>, were dismissed on suspicion of opposition activity, although there was yet insufficient evidence to have them arrested. On the civilian front, <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Bonhoeffer" title="Dietrich Bonhoeffer">Dietrich Bonhoeffer</a> was also arrested at this time, and Goerdeler was under suspicion.</p>
<p>The Gestapo had been led to Dohnanyi following the arrest of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wilhelm_Schmidhuber&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wilhelm Schmidhuber (page does not exist)">Wilhelm Schmidhuber</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Schmidhuber" title="de:Wilhelm Schmidhuber">de</a>)</span>, who had helped Dohnanyi with information and with smuggling Jews out of Germany. Under interrogation, Schmidhuber gave the Gestapo details of the Oster-Dohnanyi group in the Abwehr and also Goerdeler and Beck's involvement in opposition activities. The Gestapo reported all this to Himmler, with the observation that Canaris must be protecting Oster and Dohnanyi and the recommendation that he be arrested. Himmler passed the file back with the note "Kindly leave Canaris alone."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Either Himmler felt Canaris was too powerful to tackle at this stage, or he wanted him and his oppositional network protected for reasons of his own. Nevertheless, Oster's usefulness to the resistance was now greatly reduced. But the Gestapo did not have information about the full workings of the resistance. Most importantly, they did not know about the resistance networks based on Army Group Centre or the Bendlerblock.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, the disaster at Stalingrad, which cost Germany 400,000 casualties, was sending waves of horror and grief through German society, but causing remarkably little reduction in the people’s faith in Hitler and in Germany’s ultimate victory. This was a source of great frustration to the military and civil service plotters, who virtually all came from the elite and had privileged access to information, giving them a much greater appreciation of the hopelessness of Germany’s situation than was possessed by the German people.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_White_Rose">The White Rose</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: The White Rose">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The only visible manifestation of opposition to the regime following Stalingrad was the spontaneous action of few university students who denounced the war and the persecution and mass murder of Jews in the east. They were organised in the <a href="/wiki/White_Rose" title="White Rose">White Rose</a> group, which was centered in Munich but had connections in Berlin, Hamburg, Stuttgart and Vienna. In January 1943 they launched an anti-Nazi campaign of handbills and graffiti in and around <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Maximilian_University_of_Munich" title="Ludwig Maximilian University of Munich">Ludwig Maximilians University</a> in Munich. They were detected and some arrested. Three members, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Scholl" title="Hans Scholl">Hans Scholl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sophie_Scholl" title="Sophie Scholl">Sophie Scholl</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Probst" title="Christoph Probst">Christoph Probst</a> were to stand trial before the Nazi <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Court_(Germany)" title="People's Court (Germany)">"People's Court"</a>, where on 22 February 1943 the President of the court, <a href="/wiki/Roland_Freisler" title="Roland Freisler">Roland Freisler</a>, sentenced them to death. They were guillotined that same day at <a href="/wiki/Stadelheim_Prison" title="Stadelheim Prison">Stadelheim Prison</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Huber" title="Kurt Huber">Kurt Huber</a>, a professor of philosophy and musicology, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Schmorell" title="Alexander Schmorell">Alexander Schmorell</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Willi_Graf" title="Willi Graf">Willi Graf</a> had to stand trial later and sentenced to death as well, whereas many others were sentenced to prison terms. The last member to be executed was <a href="/wiki/Hans_Conrad_Leipelt" title="Hans Conrad Leipelt">Hans Conrad Leipelt</a> on 29 January 1945.</p>
<p>This outbreak was surprising and worrying to the Nazi regime, because the universities had been strongholds of Nazi sentiment even before Hitler had come to power. Similarly, it gave heart to the scattered and demoralised resistance groups. But White Rose was not a sign of widespread civilian disaffection from the regime, and had no imitators elsewhere, although their sixth leaflet, re-titled "The Manifesto of the Students of Munich", was dropped by Allied planes in July 1943, and became widely known in World War II Germany. The underground SPD and KPD were able to maintain their networks, and reported increasing discontent at the course of the war and at the resultant economic hardship, particularly among the industrial workers and among farmers (who suffered from the acute shortage of labour with so many young men away at the front). But there was nothing approaching active hostility to the regime. Most Germans continued to revere Hitler and blamed Himmler or other subordinates for their troubles. And from late 1943 fear of the advancing Soviets and prospects of a military offensive from the Western Powers eclipsed resentment at the regime and if anything hardened the will to resist the advancing allies.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Unorganized_resistance">Unorganized resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Unorganized resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ehrenfeld_Gedenktafel_Edelwei%C3%9Fpiraten.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="640" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Ehrenfeld_Gedenktafel_Edelwei%C3%9Fpiraten.jpg/220px-Ehrenfeld_Gedenktafel_Edelwei%C3%9Fpiraten.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Ehrenfeld_Gedenktafel_Edelwei%C3%9Fpiraten.jpg/330px-Ehrenfeld_Gedenktafel_Edelwei%C3%9Fpiraten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Ehrenfeld_Gedenktafel_Edelwei%C3%9Fpiraten.jpg/440px-Ehrenfeld_Gedenktafel_Edelwei%C3%9Fpiraten.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ehrenfeld_Gedenktafel_Edelwei%C3%9Fpiraten.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Memorial to the "Edelweisspiraten" youth group, six of whom were hanged in Cologne in 1944</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It cannot be disputed that many Germans supported the regime until the end of the war. But beneath the surface of German society there were also currents of resistance, if not always consciously political. The German historian <a href="/wiki/Detlev_Peukert" title="Detlev Peukert">Detlev Peukert</a>, who pioneered the study of German society during the Nazi era, called this phenomenon "everyday resistance." His research was based partly on the regular reports by the Gestapo and the SD on morale and public opinion, and on the "Reports on Germany" which were produced by the exiled SPD based on information from its underground network in Germany and which were acknowledged to be very well informed.</p>
<p>Peukert and other writers have shown that the most persistent sources of dissatisfaction in Nazi Germany were the state of the economy and anger at the corruption of Nazi Party officials — although these rarely affected the personal popularity of Hitler himself. The Nazi regime is frequently credited with "curing unemployment," but this was done mainly by conscription and rearmament — the civilian economy remained weak throughout the Nazi period. Although prices were fixed by law, wages remained low and there were acute shortages, particularly once the war started. To this after 1942 was added the acute misery caused by Allied air attacks on German cities. The high living and venality of Nazi officials such as <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann Göring">Hermann Göring</a> aroused increasing anger. The result was "deep dissatisfaction among the population of all parts of the country, caused by failings in the economy, government intrusions into private life, disruption of accepted tradition and custom, and police-state controls."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Opposition based on this widespread dissatisfaction usually took "passive" forms — absenteeism, malingering, spreading rumours, trading on the black market, hoarding, avoiding various forms of state service such as donations to Nazi causes. But sometimes it took more active forms, such as warning people about to be arrested, hiding them or helping them to escape, or turning a blind eye to oppositionist activities. Among the industrial working class, where the underground SPD and KPD networks were always active, there were frequent if short-lived strikes. These were generally tolerated, at least before the outbreak of war, provided the demands of the strikers were purely economic and not political.</p>
<p>Another form of resistance was assisting the persecuted German Jews. By mid-1942 the deportation of German and Austrian Jews to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extermination_camps" title="Extermination camps">extermination camps</a> in Poland was well under way. It is argued by some writers that the great majority of Germans were indifferent to the fate of the Jews, and a substantial proportion actively supported the Nazi programme of extermination<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> But a minority persisted in trying to help Jews, even in the face of serious risk to themselves and their families. This was easiest in Berlin (where in any case the Jews were progressively concentrated by the regime), and easiest for wealthy and well-connected people, particularly women.</p>
<p>Aristocrats such as <a href="/wiki/Maria_von_Maltzan" title="Maria von Maltzan">Maria von Maltzan</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Marie_Therese_von_Hammerstein&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Marie Therese von Hammerstein (page does not exist)">Marie Therese von Hammerstein</a> obtained papers for Jews and helped many to escape from Germany. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wieblingen" title="Wieblingen">Wieblingen</a> in Baden, <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_von_Thadden" title="Elisabeth von Thadden">Elisabeth von Thadden</a>, a private girls' school principal, disregarded official edicts and continued to enroll Jewish girls at her school until May 1941 when the school was nationalised and she was dismissed (she was executed in 1944, following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frau_Solf_Tea_Party" title="Frau Solf Tea Party">Frau Solf Tea Party</a>). A Berlin Protestant Minister, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Gr%C3%BCber" title="Heinrich Grüber">Heinrich Grüber</a>, organised the smuggling of Jews to the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>. At the Foreign Office, Canaris conspired to send a number of Jews to Switzerland under various pretexts. It is estimated that 2,000 Jews were hidden in Berlin until the end of the war. <a href="/wiki/Martin_Gilbert" title="Martin Gilbert">Martin Gilbert</a> has documented numerous cases of Germans and Austrians, including officials and Army officers, who saved the lives of Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rosenstrasse_Berlin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Rosenstrasse_Berlin.jpg/220px-Rosenstrasse_Berlin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Rosenstrasse_Berlin.jpg/330px-Rosenstrasse_Berlin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Rosenstrasse_Berlin.jpg/440px-Rosenstrasse_Berlin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rosenstrasse_Berlin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Rosenstrasse, where the only public protest against the deportation of German Jews took place in 1943</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There was only one public manifestation of opposition to the Nazi persecution of the German Jews, the <a href="/wiki/Rosenstrasse_protest" title="Rosenstrasse protest">Rosenstrasse protest</a> of February 1943, sparked by the arrest and threatened deportation to death camps of 1,800 Jewish men married to non-Jewish women. Before these men could be deported, their wives and other relatives rallied outside the building in Rosenstrasse where the men were held. About 6,000 people, mostly women, rallied in shifts in the winter cold for over a week. Eventually Himmler, worried about the effect on civilian morale, gave in and allowed the arrested men to be released. Some who had already been deported and were on their way to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auschwitz" title="Auschwitz">Auschwitz</a> were brought back. There was no retaliation against the protesters, and most of the Jewish men survived the war. This incident was remarkable both for its success and its uniqueness, and again raises the question of what might have happened if more Germans had been willing to protest against the deportations.</p>
<p>Nazism had a powerful appeal to German youth, particularly middle-class youth, and German universities were strongholds of Nazism even before Hitler came to power. The <a href="/wiki/Hitler_Youth" title="Hitler Youth">Hitler Youth</a> sought to mobilise all young Germans behind the regime, and apart from stubborn resistance in some rural Catholic areas, was generally successful in the first period of Nazi rule. After about 1938, however, persistent alienation among some sections of German youth began to appear. This rarely took the form of overt political opposition — the <a href="/wiki/White_Rose" title="White Rose">White Rose</a> group was a striking exception, but was striking mainly for its uniqueness. Much more common was what would now be called "dropping out" — a passive refusal to take part in official youth culture and a search for alternatives. Although none of the unofficial youth groups amounted to a serious threat to the Nazi regime, and although they provided no aid or comfort to those groups within the German elite who were actively plotting against Hitler, they do serve to show that there were currents of opposition at other levels of German society.</p>
<p>Examples were the so-called <i><a href="/wiki/Edelweiss_Pirates" title="Edelweiss Pirates">Edelweisspiraten</a></i> ("Edelweiss Pirates"), a loose network of working-class youth groups in a number of cities, who held unauthorised meetings and engaged in street fights with the Hitler Youth; the <a href="/wiki/Leipzig_Meuten" title="Leipzig Meuten">Meuten</a> group in <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a>, a more politicised group with links to the KPD underground, which had more than a thousand members in the late 1930s; and, most notably, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swing_Kids" title="Swing Kids">Swingjugend</a></i>, middle-class youth who met in secret clubs in Berlin and most other large cities to listen to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swing_(genre)" title="Swing (genre)">swing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> and other music deemed "degenerate" by the Nazi authorities. This movement, which involved distinctive forms of dress and gradually become more consciously political, became so popular that it provoked a crackdown: in 1941 Himmler ordered the arrest of Swing activists and had some sent to concentration camps.</p>
<p>In October 1944, as the American and British armies approached the western borders of Germany, there was a serious outbreak of disorder in the bomb-ravaged city of <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, which had been largely evacuated. The <i>Edelweisspiraten</i> linked up with gangs of deserters, escaped prisoners and foreign workers, and the underground KPD network, to engage in looting and sabotage, and the assassination of Gestapo and Nazi Party officials. Explosives were stolen with the objective of blowing up the Gestapo headquarters. Himmler, fearing the resistance would spread to other cities as the Allied armies advanced into Germany, ordered a savage crackdown, and for days gunbattles raged in the ruined streets of Cologne. More than 200 people were arrested and dozens were hanged in public, among them six teenaged <i>Edelweisspiraten</i>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bartholom%C3%A4us_Schink" title="Bartholomäus Schink">Bartholomäus Schink</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_Allies">Relations with Allies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Relations with Allies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The various groups of German resistance against Nazi government had different attitudes to the Allies. The most visible resistance group of the July 20 plot wasn't interested in dealing with all the Allies, and pressed demands against such Allied countries as Poland and the Soviet Union; some of its members were involved in atrocities against people in these countries. In particular the July 20th plotters demanded in their proposals to occupy Poland and annex its territory, while occupying the rest of East Europe and continuing war with the Soviet Union. The token representative of the July 20 Group, Claus von Stauffenberg, was known for his support towards German colonization of Poland as well as racist remarks regarding Polish Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martyn_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martyn-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many postwar German commentators blamed the allies for having isolated the resistance with their demand of unconditional surrender, while ignoring that the resistance offered unrealistic demands towards the Allies. While English historians too have criticized the unconditional surrender, most of them agree that it had no real impact on the final outcome of the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Prior to the formulation of unconditional surrender by the Allies, the peace demands sent from the German resistance were hardly satisfactory; for example in 1941 a proposal by Goerdeler demanded borders of 1914 with France, Belgium and Poland, as well as acceptance of annexation of Austria and Sudetenland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Getting_History_Right.27_page_120_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-.27Getting_History_Right.27_page_120-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>While German popular memory and public discourse portrays the resistance as isolated due to demand of unconditional surrender, in reality its isolation was due to unrealistic expectations of what the Allies would accept; while German commentators write that the resistance tried "to save that which remained to be saved", they omit the fact that it included a significant portion of territories conquered by Nazi Germany from its neighbours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Getting_History_Right.27_page_120_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-.27Getting_History_Right.27_page_120-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SHAEF" title="SHAEF">SHAEF</a> directive prohibited activities aimed at promoting German revolt against the Nazi regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Allied doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Unconditional_surrender" title="Unconditional surrender">unconditional surrender</a> meant that "... those Germans — and particularly those German generals — who might have been ready to throw Hitler over, and were able to do so, were discouraged from making the attempt by their inability to extract from the Allies any sort of assurance that such action would improve the treatment meted out to their country."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 11 December, <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Strategic_Services" title="Office of Strategic Services">OSS</a> operative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Joseph_Donovan" title="William Joseph Donovan">William Donovan</a> sent U.S. President Roosevelt a telegraph message from Bern, warning him of the consequences that the knowledge of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morgenthau_plan" title="Morgenthau plan">Morgenthau plan</a> had had on German resistance; by showing them that the enemy planned the enslavement of Germany it had welded together ordinary Germans and the regime; the Germans continue to fight because they are convinced that defeat will bring nothing but oppression and exploitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The message was a translation of a recent article in the <i><a href="/wiki/Neue_Z%C3%BCrcher_Zeitung" title="Neue Zürcher Zeitung">Neue Zürcher Zeitung</a></i>.</p>
<blockquote>
<p>So far, the Allies have not offered the opposition any serious encouragement. On the contrary, they have again and again welded together the people and the Nazis by statements published, either out of indifference or with a purpose. To take a recent example, the Morgenthau plan gave Dr. Goebbels the best possible chance. He was able to prove to his countrymen, in black and white, that the enemy planned the enslavement of Germany. The conviction that Germany had nothing to expect from defeat but oppression and exploitation still prevails, and that accounts for the fact that the Germans continue to fight. It is not a question of a regime, but of the homeland itself, and to save that, every German is bound to obey the call, whether he be Nazi or member of the opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>On 20 July 1945 — the first anniversary of the failed attempt to kill Hitler — no mention whatsoever was made of the event. This was because reminding the German population of the fact that there had been active German resistance to Hitler would undermine the Allied efforts to instill a sense of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collective_guilt" title="Collective guilt">collective guilt</a> in the German populace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> (See also <a href="/wiki/Denazification" title="Denazification">Denazification</a>)</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Towards_July_20">Towards July 20</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Towards July 20">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-022-2948-19,_Russland,_Unternehmen_%22Zitadelle%22._Soldatengrab.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="792" data-file-width="508" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-022-2948-19%2C_Russland%2C_Unternehmen_%22Zitadelle%22._Soldatengrab.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-022-2948-19%2C_Russland%2C_Unternehmen_%22Zitadelle%22._Soldatengrab.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-022-2948-19%2C_Russland%2C_Unternehmen_%22Zitadelle%22._Soldatengrab.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-022-2948-19%2C_Russland%2C_Unternehmen_%22Zitadelle%22._Soldatengrab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-022-2948-19%2C_Russland%2C_Unternehmen_%22Zitadelle%22._Soldatengrab.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-022-2948-19%2C_Russland%2C_Unternehmen_%22Zitadelle%22._Soldatengrab.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-022-2948-19,_Russland,_Unternehmen_%22Zitadelle%22._Soldatengrab.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The grave of a German soldier, Heinz Kühl, on the <a href="/wiki/Kursk" title="Kursk">Kursk</a> battlefield</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By mid-1943 the tide of war was turning decisively against Germany. The last major German offensive on the Eastern Front, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kursk" title="Battle of Kursk">Operation Citadel</a>, ended in the defeat for the Germans at <a href="/wiki/Kursk" title="Kursk">Kursk</a>, and in July <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Mussolini</a> was overthrown. The Army and civilian plotters became more convinced than ever that Hitler must be assassinated so that a government acceptable to the western Allies could be formed and a separate peace negotiated in time to prevent a Soviet invasion of Germany. This scenario, while more credible than some of the resistance’s earlier plans, was based on a <a href="/wiki/False_premise" title="False premise">false premise</a>: that the western Allies would be willing to break with Stalin and negotiate a separate peace with a non-Nazi German government. In fact both <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Churchill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Roosevelt</a> were committed to the “unconditional surrender” formula.</p>
<p>Since the Foreign Office was a stronghold of resistance activists, it was not difficult for the conspirators to reach the Allies via diplomats in neutral countries. Theo Kordt, based in the German Embassy in <a href="/wiki/Bern" title="Bern">Bern</a>, and advised by the Foreign Officers resisters Ulrich von Hassell and Adam von Trott zu Solz, communicated with the British via intermediaries such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Willem_Visser%E2%80%99t_Hooft" title="Willem Visser’t Hooft">Willem Visser’t Hooft</a>, secretary-general of the <a href="/wiki/World_Council_of_Churches" title="World Council of Churches">World Council of Churches</a>, based in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>. The Kreisau Circle sent <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Bonhoeffer" title="Dietrich Bonhoeffer">Dietrich Bonhoeffer</a> and Helmut von Moltke to meet <a href="/wiki/George_Bell_(bishop)" title="George Bell (bishop)">George Bell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Chichester" title="Bishop of Chichester">Bishop of Chichester</a>, at a church conference in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>. Bell passed their messages and plans on to Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Eden" title="Anthony Eden">Anthony Eden</a>. An American journalist, <a href="/wiki/Louis_P._Lochner" title="Louis P. Lochner">Louis P. Lochner</a>, carried coded messages out of Germany and took them to Roosevelt. Other envoys worked through Vatican channels, or via diplomats in <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> – a recognised site for indirect communication between Germany and the Allied countries.</p>
<p>All of these overtures were rejected, and indeed they were usually simply ignored. The western Allies would give the German resistance no assistance or even recognition. There were several reasons for this. First, they did not know or trust the resisters, who seemed to them to be a clique of Prussian reactionaries concerned mainly to save their own skins now that Germany was losing the war. This attitude was encouraged by visceral anti-Germans such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Gilbert_Vansittart" title="Robert Gilbert Vansittart">Lord Vansittart</a>, Churchill’s diplomatic adviser, who regarded all Germans as evil. Second, Roosevelt and Churchill were both acutely aware that the Soviet Union was bearing the brunt of the war against Hitler, and were aware of Stalin’s constant suspicions that they were doing deals behind his back. They thus refused any discussions that might be seen as suggesting a willingness to reach a separate peace with Germany. Third, the Allies were determined that in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, unlike in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, Germany must be comprehensively defeated in the field so that another <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dolchstosslegende" title="Dolchstosslegende">"stab in the back” myth</a> would not to arise in Germany.</p>
<p>Olbricht now put forward a new strategy for staging a coup against Hitler. The Reserve Army had an operational plan called Operation Valkyrie, which was to be used if the disruption caused by the Allied bombing of German cities caused a breakdown in law and order, or a rising by the millions of slave labourers from occupied countries now being used in German factories. Olbricht suggested that this plan could be used to mobilise the Reserve Army to stage a coup. In the autumn of 1943, Tresckow revised Valkyrie plan and drafted supplemental orders to take control of German cities, disarm the SS and arrest the Nazi leadership after Hitler's assassination. Operation Valkyrie could only be put into effect by General <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Fromm" title="Friedrich Fromm">Friedrich Fromm</a>, commander of the Reserve Army, so he must either be won over to the conspiracy or in some way neutralised if the plan was to succeed. Fromm, like many senior officers, knew about the military conspiracies against Hitler but neither supported them nor reported them to the Gestapo.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Claus_Schenk_Graf_von_Stauffenberg_-_Helmuth_James_Graf_von_Moltke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="418" data-file-width="677" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Claus_Schenk_Graf_von_Stauffenberg_-_Helmuth_James_Graf_von_Moltke.jpg/220px-Claus_Schenk_Graf_von_Stauffenberg_-_Helmuth_James_Graf_von_Moltke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Claus_Schenk_Graf_von_Stauffenberg_-_Helmuth_James_Graf_von_Moltke.jpg/330px-Claus_Schenk_Graf_von_Stauffenberg_-_Helmuth_James_Graf_von_Moltke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Claus_Schenk_Graf_von_Stauffenberg_-_Helmuth_James_Graf_von_Moltke.jpg/440px-Claus_Schenk_Graf_von_Stauffenberg_-_Helmuth_James_Graf_von_Moltke.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Claus_Schenk_Graf_von_Stauffenberg_-_Helmuth_James_Graf_von_Moltke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A German stamp of <a href="/wiki/Claus_von_Stauffenberg" title="Claus von Stauffenberg">Claus Philipp Maria Schenk Graf von Stauffenberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helmuth_James_Graf_von_Moltke" title="Helmuth James Graf von Moltke">Helmuth James Graf von Moltke</a> in commemoration of their 100th birthdays.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In August 1943 Tresckow met a young staff officer, Colonel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claus_Schenk_Graf_von_Stauffenberg" title="Claus Schenk Graf von Stauffenberg">Claus Schenk Graf von Stauffenberg</a>, for the first time. Badly wounded in North Africa, Stauffenberg was a devout Catholic, a political conservative and a zealous German nationalist with a taste for philosophy. He had at first welcomed the Nazi regime but had become rapidly disillusioned. By 1942 he shared the widespread conviction among Army officers that Germany was being led to disaster and that Hitler must be removed from power. For some time his religious scruples had prevented him from coming to the conclusion that assassination was the correct way to achieve this. After Stalingrad, however, he decided that <i>not</i> assassinating Hitler would be a greater moral evil.</p>
<p>During late 1943 and early 1944 there were a series of attempts to get one of the military conspirators near enough to Hitler for long enough to kill him with a bomb or a revolver. But the task was becoming increasingly difficult. As the war situation deteriorated, Hitler no longer appeared in public and rarely visited Berlin. He spent most of his time at his headquarters in East Prussia, with occasional breaks at his Bavarian mountain retreat in <a href="/wiki/Berchtesgaden" title="Berchtesgaden">Berchtesgaden</a>. In both places he was heavily guarded and rarely saw people he did not already know and trust. Himmler and the Gestapo were increasingly suspicious of plots against Hitler, and specifically suspected the officers of the General Staff, which was indeed the place where most of the young officers willing to sacrifice themselves to kill Hitler were located. All these attempts therefore failed, sometimes by a matter of minutes.</p>
<p>Further blows came in January and February 1944 when first Moltke and then Canaris were arrested. By the summer of 1944 the Gestapo was closing in on the conspirators. On 4 July <a href="/wiki/Julius_Leber" title="Julius Leber">Julius Leber</a>, who was trying to establish contact between his own underground SPD network and the KPD’s network in the interests of the “united front,” was arrested after attending a meeting which had been infiltrated by the Gestapo. There was a sense that time was running out, both on the battlefield, where the eastern front was in full retreat and where the Allies had landed in France on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D-Day" title="D-Day">6 June</a>, and in Germany, where the resistance’s room for manoeuvre was rapidly contracting. The belief that this was the last chance for action seized the conspirators. Few now believed that the Allies would agree to a separate peace with a non-Nazi government, even if Hitler was assassinated. Leber in particular had argued that “unconditional surrender” was inevitable and the only question was whether it would be before or after the Soviets invaded Germany.</p>
<p>By this time the core of the conspirators had begun to think of themselves as doomed men, whose actions were more symbolic than real. The purpose of the conspiracy was seen by some of them as saving the honour of themselves, their families, the Army and Germany through a grand, if futile, gesture, rather than altering the course of history. Tresckow said to Stauffenberg through one of his aides, Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Graf_von_Lehndorff-Steinort" title="Heinrich Graf von Lehndorff-Steinort">Heinrich Graf von Lehndorff-Steinort</a>: “The assassination must be attempted, <i>coûte que coûte</i> [whatever the cost]. Even if it fails, we must take action in Berlin. For the practical purpose no longer matters; what matters now is that the German resistance movement must take the plunge before the eyes of the world and of history. Compared to that, nothing else matters.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146III-347,_Werner_Karl_v._Haeften.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="565" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146III-347%2C_Werner_Karl_v._Haeften.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146III-347%2C_Werner_Karl_v._Haeften.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146III-347%2C_Werner_Karl_v._Haeften.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146III-347%2C_Werner_Karl_v._Haeften.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146III-347%2C_Werner_Karl_v._Haeften.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146III-347%2C_Werner_Karl_v._Haeften.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146III-347,_Werner_Karl_v._Haeften.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Werner_von_Haeften" title="Werner von Haeften">Werner Karl von Haeften</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In retrospect it is surprising that these months of plotting by the resistance groups in the Army and the state apparatus, in which dozens of people were involved and of which many more, including very senior Army officers, were aware, apparently totally escaped the attentions of the Gestapo. In fact, as was noted earlier, the Gestapo had known since February 1943 of both the Abwehr resistance group under the patronage of Canaris and of the Goedeler-Beck circle. If all these people had been arrested and interrogated, the Gestapo might well have uncovered the group based in Army Group Centre as well and the July 20 assassination attempt would never have happened. This raises the possibility that Himmler knew about the plot and, for reasons of his own, allowed it to go ahead.</p>
