<p>Himmler had had at least one conversation with a known oppositionist when, in August 1943, the Prussian Finance Minister Johannes Popitz came to see him and offered him the support of the opposition if he would make a move to displace Hitler and secure a negotiated end to the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Nothing came of this meeting, but Popitz was not arrested and Himmler apparently did nothing to track down the resistance network which he knew was operating within the state bureaucracy. It is possible that Himmler, who by late 1943 knew that the war was unwinnable, allowed the July 20 plot to go ahead in the knowledge that if it succeeded he would be Hitler's successor, and could then lead to a peace settlement. Popitz was not alone in seeing in Himmler a potential ally. General von Bock advised Tresckow to seek his support, but there is no evidence that he did so. Gordeler was apparently also in indirect contact with Himmler via a mutual acquaintance <a href="/wiki/Carl_Langbehn" title="Carl Langbehn">Carl Langbehn</a>. Canaris's biographer <a href="/wiki/Heinz_H%C3%B6hne" title="Heinz Höhne">Heinz Höhne</a> suggests that Canaris and Himmler were working together to bring about a change of regime. All of this remains speculation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Himmler in fact knew more about the real level of opposition to the Nazi regime than did the opposition itself. To the resistance activists it seemed that the German people continued to place their faith in Hitler no matter how dire the military and economic situation had become. But Himmler was receiving regular reports from the <a href="/wiki/Sicherheitsdienst" title="Sicherheitsdienst">SD</a> (Security Service, the intelligence arm of the SS), about the real state of German morale. These were compiled by SS-Gruppenfüher <a href="/wiki/Otto_Ohlendorf" title="Otto Ohlendorf">Otto Ohlendorf</a> and were drawn from the SD's wide range of contacts all over Germany. They showed a sharp decline in civilian morale and in the level of support for the Nazi regime, beginning after Stalingrad and accelerating through 1943 as the military setbacks continued, the economic situation deteriorated and the Allied bombing of German cities grew more intense. By the end of 1943 Himmler knew that most Germans no longer believed that war could be won and that many, perhaps a majority, had lost faith in Hitler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> But fear of the Gestapo meant that this disillusionment did not translate into political opposition to the regime — even though, as the <a href="/wiki/Rosenstrasse_protest" title="Rosenstrasse protest">Rosenstrasse protest</a> showed, it was possible even as late as 1943 for courageous opponents of Nazi policies to make public and successful protests.</p>
<p>Nevertheless, organised resistance begun to stir during 1944. While the SPD and KPD trade unions had been destroyed in 1933, the Catholic unions had voluntarily dissolved along with the <a href="/wiki/Centre_Party_(Germany)" title="Centre Party (Germany)">Centre Party</a>. As a result, Catholic unionists had been less zealously repressed than their socialist counterparts, and had maintained an informal network of activists. Their leaders, <a href="/wiki/Jakob_Kaiser" title="Jakob Kaiser">Jakob Kaiser</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Max_Habermann&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Max Habermann (page does not exist)">Max Habermann</a>, judged by the beginning of 1944 that it was time to take action. They organised a network of resistance cells in government offices across Germany, ready to rise and take control of their buildings when the word was given by the military that Hitler was dead.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="20_July_plot">20 July plot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 20 July plot">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/20_July_plot" title="20 July plot">20 July plot</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proposed_German_National_Flag_1948.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1100" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Proposed_German_National_Flag_1948.svg/200px-Proposed_German_National_Flag_1948.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Proposed_German_National_Flag_1948.svg/300px-Proposed_German_National_Flag_1948.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Proposed_German_National_Flag_1948.svg/400px-Proposed_German_National_Flag_1948.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:German_Resistance_Flag_Proposal_1944.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="500" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/German_Resistance_Flag_Proposal_1944.svg/200px-German_Resistance_Flag_Proposal_1944.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/German_Resistance_Flag_Proposal_1944.svg/300px-German_Resistance_Flag_Proposal_1944.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/German_Resistance_Flag_Proposal_1944.svg/400px-German_Resistance_Flag_Proposal_1944.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><img alt="" data-file-height="150" data-file-width="230" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/FIAV_proposal.svg/23px-FIAV_proposal.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/FIAV_proposal.svg/35px-FIAV_proposal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/FIAV_proposal.svg/46px-FIAV_proposal.svg.png 2x" title="Design was proposed in the past, but never officially adopted" width="23"/> Two variants of <a href="/wiki/Josef_Wirmer" title="Josef Wirmer">Josef Wirmer</a>'s 1944 "Resistance" design, created by his brother, Ernst. The top flag was proposed by conservative parties as a flag for West Germany (1948).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bendelerblock.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Bendelerblock.jpg/220px-Bendelerblock.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Bendelerblock.jpg/330px-Bendelerblock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Bendelerblock.jpg/440px-Bendelerblock.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bendelerblock.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The courtyard at the Bendlerblock, where Stauffenberg, Olbricht and others were executed</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 1 July Stauffenberg was appointed chief-of-staff to General Fromm at the Reserve Army headquarters on Bendlerstrasse in central Berlin. This position enabled Stauffenberg to attend Hitler’s military conferences, either in East Prussia or at Berchtesgaden, and would thus give him a golden opportunity, perhaps the last that would present itself, to kill Hitler with a bomb or a pistol. Conspirators who had long resisted on moral grounds the idea of killing Hitler now changed their minds – partly because they were hearing reports of the mass murder at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auschwitz" title="Auschwitz">Auschwitz</a> of up to 400,000 Hungarian Jews, the culmination of the Nazi <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocaust" title="Holocaust">Holocaust</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Meanwhile, new key allies had been gained. These included General <a href="/wiki/Carl-Heinrich_von_St%C3%BClpnagel" title="Carl-Heinrich von Stülpnagel">Carl-Heinrich von Stülpnagel</a>, the German military commander in France, who would take control in Paris when Hitler was killed and, it was hoped, negotiate an immediate armistice with the invading Allied armies. The demands of the plotters regarding armistice with Allies included Germany retaining its 1914 eastern borders, including the Polish territories of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wielkopolska" title="Wielkopolska">Wielkopolska</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84" title="Poznań">Poznań</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Other demands included keeping such territorial gains as <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sudetenland" title="Sudetenland">Sudetenland</a> within the Reich, giving autonomy to <a href="/wiki/Alsace-Lorraine" title="Alsace-Lorraine">Alsace-Lorraine</a>, and even expansion of the current wartime borders of Germany in the south by annexing Tyrol as far as <a href="/wiki/Bolzano" title="Bolzano">Bolzano</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merano" title="Merano">Merano</a>. Non-territorial demands included such points as refusal of any occupation of Germany by the Allies, as well as refusal to hand over war criminals by demanding the right of "nations to deal with their own criminals". These proposals were only directed to the Western Allies – Stauffenberg wanted Germany only to retreat from western, southern and northern positions, while demanding the right to continue military occupation of German territorial gains in the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martyn2_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martyn2-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>The plot was now as ready as it would ever be. Twice in early July Stauffenberg attended Hitler's conferences carrying a bomb in his briefcase. But because the conspirators had decided that Himmler, too, must be assassinated if the planned mobilisation of Operation Valkyrie was to have any chance of success, he had held back at the last minute because Himmler was not present – in fact it was unusual for Himmler to attend military conferences. By 15 July, when Stauffenberg again flew to East Prussia, this condition had been dropped. The plan was for Stauffenberg to plant the briefcase with the bomb in Hitler's conference room with a timer running, excuse himself from the meeting, wait for the explosion, then fly back to Berlin and join the other plotters at the Bendlerblock. Operation Valkyrie would be mobilised, the Reserve Army would take control of Germany and the other Nazi leaders would be arrested. Beck would be appointed head of state, Gordeler Chancellor and Witzleben commander-in-chief. The plan was ambitious and depended on a run of very good luck, but it was not totally fanciful.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rastenburg">Rastenburg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Rastenburg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-025-10,_Hitler-Attentat,_20._Juli_1944.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="799" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-025-10%2C_Hitler-Attentat%2C_20._Juli_1944.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-025-10%2C_Hitler-Attentat%2C_20._Juli_1944.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-025-10%2C_Hitler-Attentat%2C_20._Juli_1944.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-025-10%2C_Hitler-Attentat%2C_20._Juli_1944.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-025-10%2C_Hitler-Attentat%2C_20._Juli_1944.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-025-10%2C_Hitler-Attentat%2C_20._Juli_1944.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-109-19A,_Berlin,_Soldaten_und_Waffen-SS_im_%22Bendlerblock%22.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="527" data-file-width="800" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-109-19A%2C_Berlin%2C_Soldaten_und_Waffen-SS_im_%22Bendlerblock%22.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-109-19A%2C_Berlin%2C_Soldaten_und_Waffen-SS_im_%22Bendlerblock%22.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-109-19A%2C_Berlin%2C_Soldaten_und_Waffen-SS_im_%22Bendlerblock%22.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-109-19A%2C_Berlin%2C_Soldaten_und_Waffen-SS_im_%22Bendlerblock%22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-109-19A%2C_Berlin%2C_Soldaten_und_Waffen-SS_im_%22Bendlerblock%22.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-109-19A%2C_Berlin%2C_Soldaten_und_Waffen-SS_im_%22Bendlerblock%22.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-109-19A,_Berlin,_Soldaten_und_Waffen-SS_im_%22Bendlerblock%22.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Soldiers and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waffen_SS" title="Waffen SS">Waffen SS</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Bendlerblock" title="Bendlerblock">Bendlerblock</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Again on 15 July the attempt was called off at the last minute, for reasons which are not known because all the participants in the phone conversations which led to the postponement were dead by the end of the year. Stauffenberg, depressed and angry, returned to Berlin. On 18 July rumours reached him that the Gestapo had wind of the conspiracy and that he might be arrested at any time – this was apparently not true, but there was a sense that the net was closing in and that the next opportunity to kill Hitler must be taken because there might not be another. At 10:00 hours on 20 July Stauffenberg flew back to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wolfsschanze" title="Wolfsschanze">Wolfsschanze</a> for another Hitler military conference, again with a bomb in his briefcase. It is remarkable in retrospect that despite Hitler’s mania for security, officers attending his conferences were not searched.</p>
<p>At about 12:10 the conference began. Stauffenberg, having previously activated the timer on the bomb, placed his briefcase under the table around which Hitler and more than 20 officers were seated or standing. After ten minutes, he made an excuse and left the room. At 12:40 the bomb went off, demolishing the conference room. Several officers were killed, but not Hitler. Possibly he had been saved because the heavy oak leg of the conference table, behind which Stauffenberg's briefcase had been left, deflected the blast. But Stauffenberg, seeing the building collapse in smoke and flame, assumed Hitler was dead, leapt into a staff car and made a dash for the airfield before the alarm could be raised. By 13:00 he was airborne.</p>
<p>By the time Stauffenberg’s plane reached Berlin at about 15:00, General <a href="/wiki/Erich_Fellgiebel" title="Erich Fellgiebel">Erich Fellgiebel</a>, an officer at Rastenburg who was in on the plot, had rung the Bendlerblock and told the plotters that Hitler had survived the explosion. This was a fatal step (literally so for Fellgiebel and many others), because the Berlin plotters immediately lost their nerve, and judged, probably correctly, that the plan to mobilise Operation Valkyrie would have no chance of succeeding once the officers of the Reserve Army knew that Hitler was alive. There was more confusion when Stauffenberg’s plane landed and he phoned from the airport to say that Hitler was dead. The Bendlerblock plotters did not know whom to believe. Finally at 16:00 Olbricht issued the orders for Operation Valkyrie to be mobilised. The vacillating General Fromm, however, phoned Keitel, who assured him that Hitler was alive, and demanded to know Stauffenberg’s whereabouts. This told Fromm that the plot had been traced to his headquarters, and that he was in mortal danger.</p>
<p>At 16:40 Stauffenberg arrived at the Bendlerblock. Fromm now changed sides and attempted to have Stauffenberg arrested, but Olbricht and Stauffenberg restrained him at gunpoint. By this time Himmler had taken charge of the situation and has issued orders countermanding Olbricht's mobilisation of Operation Valkyrie. In many places the coup was going ahead, led by officers who believed that Hitler was dead. The Propaganda Ministry on the <a href="/wiki/Wilhelmstrasse" title="Wilhelmstrasse">Wilhelmstrasse</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goebbels" title="Joseph Goebbels">Joseph Goebbels</a> inside, was surrounded by troops. In Paris Stülpnagel issued orders for the arrest of the SS and <a href="/wiki/Sicherheitsdienst" title="Sicherheitsdienst">SD</a> commanders. In Vienna, Prague and many other places troops occupied Nazi Party offices and arrested Gauleiters and SS officers.</p>
<p>The decisive moment came at 19:00, when Hitler was sufficiently recovered to make phone calls. By phone he personally empowered a loyal officer, Major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otto_Remer" title="Otto Remer">Otto Remer</a>, to regain control of the situation in Berlin. At 20:00 a furious Witzleben arrived at the Bendlerblock and had a bitter argument with Stauffenberg, who was still insisting that the coup could go ahead. Witzleben left shortly afterwards. At around this time the planned seizure of power in Paris was aborted when Kluge, who had recently been appointed commander-in-chief in the west, learned that Hitler was alive, changed sides with alacrity and had Stülpnagel arrested.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Excavated_cells_from_the_basement_of_the_Gestapo_headquarters.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Excavated_cells_from_the_basement_of_the_Gestapo_headquarters.jpg/220px-Excavated_cells_from_the_basement_of_the_Gestapo_headquarters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Excavated_cells_from_the_basement_of_the_Gestapo_headquarters.jpg/330px-Excavated_cells_from_the_basement_of_the_Gestapo_headquarters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Excavated_cells_from_the_basement_of_the_Gestapo_headquarters.jpg/440px-Excavated_cells_from_the_basement_of_the_Gestapo_headquarters.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Excavated_cells_from_the_basement_of_the_Gestapo_headquarters.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The cells of the Gestapo headquarters in Prinz-Albrecht-Strasse, where many of the July 20 plotters and other resistance activists were tortured</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The less resolute members of the conspiracy in Berlin also now began to change sides. Fighting broke out in the Bendlerblock between officers supporting and opposing the coup, and Stauffenberg was wounded. By 23:00 Fromm had regained control, hoping by a show of zealous loyalty to save his own skin. Beck, realising the game was up, shot himself – the first of many suicides in the coming days. Fromm declared that he had convened a court-martial consisting of himself, and had sentenced Olbricht, Stauffenberg and two other officers to death. At 00:10 on 21 July they were shot in the courtyard outside. Others would have been executed as well, but at 00:30 the SS led by <a href="/wiki/Otto_Skorzeny" title="Otto Skorzeny">Otto Skorzeny</a> arrived on the scene and further executions were forbidden. Fromm went off to see Goebbels to claim credit for suppressing the coup. He was immediately arrested.</p>
<p>That was the end of the German resistance. Over the coming weeks Himmler’s Gestapo, driven by a furious Hitler, rounded up nearly everyone who had had the remotest connection with the July 20 plot. The discovery of letters and diaries in the homes and offices of those arrested revealed the plots of 1938, 1939 and 1943, and this led to further rounds of arrests, including that of Halder, who finished the war in a concentration camp. Under Himmler’s new <a href="/wiki/Sippenhaft" title="Sippenhaft">Sippenhaft</a> (blood guilt) laws, all the relatives of the principal plotters were also arrested. Many people killed themselves, including Tresckow, Stülpnagel, Kluge and Rommel (under Hitler's orders).</p>
<p>Very few of the plotters tried to escape, or to deny their guilt when arrested. It was as if they felt that now that honour had been satisfied, there was nothing further to be done. Hassell, who was at home in Bavaria, returned to his office in Berlin and awaited arrest. Others turned themselves in. Some plotters did manage to get away – Gisevius to Switzerland, for example. Others survived by luck or accident. It appears that none of the conspirators implicated anyone else, even under torture. It was well into August before the Gestapo learned of the Kreisau Circle. Goerdeler was not arrested until August 12.</p>
<p>Those who survived interrogation were given perfunctory trials before the People’s Court and its bullying Nazi judge <a href="/wiki/Roland_Freisler" title="Roland Freisler">Roland Freisler</a>. Eventually some 5,000 people were arrested and about 200 were executed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> – not all of them connected with the July 20 plot, since the Gestapo used the occasion to settle scores with many other people suspected of opposition sympathies. After February 1945, when Freisler was killed in an air raid, there were no more formal trials, but as late as April, with the war weeks away from its end, Canaris’s diary was found, and many more people were implicated. Executions continued down to the last days of the war.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aktion_Rheinland">Aktion Rheinland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Aktion Rheinland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aktion_Rheinland" title="Aktion Rheinland">Aktion Rheinland</a></div>
<p>One of the final acts of resistance was <i>Aktion Rheinland</i>, an operation carried out by the resistance group in <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">Düsseldorf</a> led by Karl August Wiedenhofen. The goal was to surrender the city of Düsseldorf to the advancing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Army" title="US Army">Americans</a> without any fighting, thereby preventing further destruction. The action occurred during the latter stages of the <a href="/wiki/Encirclement" title="Encirclement">encirclement</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ruhr_Pocket" title="Ruhr Pocket">Ruhr Pocket</a>, with Wiedenhofen's group briefly taking over police headquarters on 16 April 1945. Despite the plan being betrayed (leading to the execution of five Resistance fighters), other fighters managed to reach American lines, leading to the virtually bloodless capture of the city on 17 April.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Historiographical debates on the subject on <i>Widerstand</i> have often featured intense arguments about the nature, extent and effectiveness of resistance in the Third Reich.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> In particular, debate has focused around what to define as <i>Widerstand</i> (resistance).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within both the <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">Federal Republic of Germany</a> and the <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">German Democratic Republic</a>, the memory of <i>Widerstand</i> was harnessed after 1949 as a way of providing legitimacy to the two rival German states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> In East Germany, the focus was unabashedly on celebrating the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Germany" title="Communist Party of Germany">KPD</a>, which was represented as the only anti-fascist force in Germany; non-Communist resistance was either ignored or slighted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_pages_185_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_pages_185-125">[125]</a></sup> In East Germany, historical work on the subject of <i>widerstand</i> was highly politicized and portrayed members of the KPD resistance as heroes. The general tone of East German work on the subject was well summarized by the introduction to the 1974 book <i>Die deutsche antifaschistische Widerstandsbewegung</i>, which stated: “The German anti-fascist resistance movement, especially the KPD and the forces allied to it, embodied the progressive line of German policy. The most consistent political force of this movement, the KPD, carried out from the first day of the fascist dictatorship, organized and, centrally directed the struggle against imperialism…The expression of the victory of the resolute anti-fascists after the smashing of fascism by the Soviet Union, and the other states of the Anti-Hitler coalition, and the defeat of German imperialism is the existence of the GDR in which the legacy of the best of the German people who gave their lives in the anti-fascist struggle was realized”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_pages_185_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_pages_185-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>In West Germany, the first works to appear on the subject, such as the books by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Rothfels" title="Hans Rothfels">Hans Rothfels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Ritter" title="Gerhard Ritter">Gerhard Ritter</a>, were intended both to rebut the "collective guilt" accusations against the German people by showing the existence of the "other Germany", and to prevent another <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stab-in-the-back_legend" title="Stab-in-the-back legend">Dolchstoßlegende</a></i> from emerging by portraying those involved in <i>Widerstand</i> activities in as heroic light as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Under the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, starting in the late 1940s, and continuing throughout the 1950s, historiographical work on the subject in the Federal Republic came to increasing exclude the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Germany" title="Communist Party of Germany">KPD</a>, and assigned a minor role to the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">SPD</a>. In his biography of Goerdeler, Ritter drew a distinction between those Germans working for the defeat of their country, and those Germans working to overthrow the Nazi regime while being loyal to Germany. Thus, in Ritter’s view, Goerdeler was a patriot while those involved in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Orchestra_(spy)" title="Red Orchestra (spy)">Rote Kapelle</a></i> were traitors who deserved to be executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> In general, West German historians in the 1950s came to define <i>Widerstand</i> as only including national-conservatives involved in the July 20 plot, and a "monumentalization" and "heroicization" of <i>Widerstand</i> occurred with those being involved being credited as acting from the highest possible ethical and moral motives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_187_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_187-128">[128]</a></sup> In the 1950s, resistance was depicted as middle-class and Christian with the emphasis on the heroic individual standing alone against tyranny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_187_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_187-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Starting in the 1960s, a younger generation of West German historians such as <a href="/wiki/Hans_Mommsen" title="Hans Mommsen">Hans Mommsen</a> started to provide a more critical assessment of <i>Widerstand</i> within German elites, and came to decry the "monumentalization" of the 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> In two articles published in 1966, Mommsen proved the claim often advanced in the 1950s that the ideas behind "men of July 20" were the inspiration for the 1949 Basic Law of the Federal Republic was false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Mommsen showed that the ideas of national-conservative opponents of the Nazis had their origins in the anti-Weimar right of the 1920s, that the system the national-conservatives wished to build in place of Nazism was not a democracy, and that national-conservatives wished to see a "Greater Germany" ruling over much of Central and Eastern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> As part of a critical evaluation of those involved in anti-Nazi work, the German historian Christof Dipper in his 1983 essay "<i>Der Deutsche Widerstand und die Juden</i>" (translated into English as "The German Resistance and the Jews") argued that the majority of the anti-Nazi national-conservatives were anti-Semitic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marrus.2C_Michael_page_92_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marrus.2C_Michael_page_92-132">[132]</a></sup> Dipper wrote that for the majority of the national-conservatives "the bureaucratic, pseudo-legal deprivation of the Jews practiced until 1938 was still considered acceptable"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marrus.2C_Michael_page_92_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marrus.2C_Michael_page_92-132">[132]</a></sup> Though Dipper noted no-one in the <i>Widerstand</i> movement supported <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a>, he also commented that the national-conservatives did not intend to restore civil rights to the Jews after the overthrow of Hitler<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marrus.2C_Michael_page_92_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marrus.2C_Michael_page_92-132">[132]</a></sup> Dipper went on to argue that based on such views held by opponents of the regime that for "a large part of the German people...believed that a "Jewish Question" existed and had to be solved...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marrus.2C_Michael_page_92_132-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marrus.2C_Michael_page_92-132">[132]</a></sup> In response to Dipper's charges, the Canadian historian Peter Hoffmann in his 2004 essay "The German Resistance and the Holocaust" sought to disapprove Dipper's thesis. Hoffmann argued that the majority of those involved in the July 20th <i>putsch</i> attempt were motivated in large part to moral objections to the <i>Shoah</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In particular, Hoffmann used the example of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claus_Schenk_Graf_von_Stauffenberg" title="Claus Schenk Graf von Stauffenberg">Claus von Stauffenberg</a>'s moral outrage to witnessing the massacre of Russian Jews in 1942, and of <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Goerdeler" title="Carl Friedrich Goerdeler">Carl Friedrich Goerdeler</a>'s advice in 1938–39 to his contact with British intelligence, the industrialist A.P. Young that the British government should take a tough line with the Nazi regime in regards to its anti-Semitism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The Israeli historian Danny Orbach in his 2010 book Valkyrie: Hahitnagdut Hagermanit Lehitler defended the German resistance fighters, particularly Goerdeler, against the charge that they were anti-Semitic by noting Goerdeler's strong support for Zionism, the importance of the Holocaust in the motives of the National-Conservative resistance, as well as attempts of other German resistance fighters to save persecuted Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> In a recent article, Orbach also argued that Dipper's accusations of antiseminitsm are based on a misreading, if not distortion, of the primary sources, above all Goerdeler's memoranda on the Jewish Question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Josef-Schultz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="483" data-file-width="788" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/Josef-Schultz.jpg/220px-Josef-Schultz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/Josef-Schultz.jpg/330px-Josef-Schultz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/Josef-Schultz.jpg/440px-Josef-Schultz.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Josef-Schultz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The story of <a href="/wiki/Josef_Schulz" title="Josef Schulz">Josef Schulz</a> became a symbol of resistance after the war.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Increasingly, West German historians started in the 1960s and 1970s to examine <i>Widerstand</i> outside of elites, and by focusing on resistance by ordinary people to challenge the popular notion that had been "resistance without the people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> An example of the changing trend in historical research was a series of local studies of varying degrees of quality on working-class resistance movements associated with the SPD and the KPD published in the 1970s, which shed much light on these previously little known movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> As the historical genre of <i><a href="/wiki/Alltagsgeschichte" title="Alltagsgeschichte">Alltagsgeschichte</a></i> (history of everyday life) started to enjoy increasing popularity as a research topic in the 1970s–80s, historians became more preoccupied with that they considered to be "everyday" resistance by individuals acting outside of any sort of organization".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The so-called "Bavaria Project" of the 1970s, an effort made by the <a href="/wiki/Institut_f%C3%BCr_Zeitgeschichte" title="Institut für Zeitgeschichte">Institute of Contemporary History</a> to comprehensively document "everyday life" in <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a> during the Third Reich did much to spur research into this area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_192_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_192-140">[140]</a></sup> The first director of the "Bavaria Project", Peter Hüttenberger defined <i>Widerstand</i> as "every form of rebellion against at least potentially total rule within the context of asymmetrical relations of rule".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_192_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_192-140">[140]</a></sup> For Hüttenberger, "symmetrical" rule occurs when there is a "bargain" struck between the different interests of the rulers and ruled which leads more or less to a "balance"; "asymmetrical rule" occurs when there is no "bargain" and the state seeks total <i>Herrschaft</i> (domination) over the ruled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_pages_192_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_pages_192-141">[141]</a></sup> For this reason, Hüttenberger discounted the East German claim that the KPD had been engaging in anti-Nazi resistance during the Weimar Republic. Hüttenberger argued that democracy is a form of "symmetrical" rule, and therefore merely being an opposition party under a democracy does not qualify as resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_pages_192_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_pages_192-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Seen within this perspective as defined by Hüttenberger, any effort made to resist the claim of total <i>Herrschaft</i>, no matter how minor was a form of <i>Widerstand</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_193_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_193-142">[142]</a></sup> Thus, the six volumes which comprised the "Bavaria Project" edited by the project's second director, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Broszat" title="Martin Broszat">Martin Broszat</a> depicted actions such as refusal to give the Nazi salute or regularly attending church as a type of resistance. Moreover, the emphasis upon resistance in "everyday life" in the "Bavaria Project" portrayed <i>Widerstand</i> not as a total contrast between black and white, but rather in shades of grey, noting that people who often refused to behave as the Nazi regime wanted in one area often conformed in other areas; as an example the Bavarian peasants who did business with Jewish cattle dealers in the 1930s despite the efforts of the Nazi regime to stop these transactions otherwise often expressed approval of the anti-Semitic laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_193_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_193-142">[142]</a></sup> Rather than defining resistance as a matter of intention, Broszat and his associates came to define <i>Widerstand</i> as a matter of <i>Wirkung</i> (effect) as a means of blocking the Nazi regime's total claim to control all aspects of German life, regardless of whether the intentions were political or not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_194_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_194-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Realizing that not every action that blocked the Nazi regime's total claims should be considered a form of <i>Widerstand</i>, Broszat devised the controversial concept of <i>Resistenz</i> (immunity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_194_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_194-143">[143]</a></sup> By <i>Resistenz</i>, Broszat meant that certain sections of German society were reasonably able to maintain their pre-1933 value system without seeking to fundamentally challenge the Nazi regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_194_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_194-143">[143]</a></sup> The <i>Resistenz</i> concept was often criticized by other historians for seeking to change the focus from "behavior" and intentions towards the Nazi regime towards the "effect" on one's actions on the regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_195_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_195-144">[144]</a></sup> One of Broszat's leading critics, the Swiss historian Walter Hofer commented that in his view: "The concept of <i>Resistenz</i> leads to a levelling down of fundamental resistance against the system on the one hand and actions criticizing more or less accidental, superficial manifestations on the other: the tyrannicide appears on the same plane as the illegal cattle-slaughterer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_195_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_195-144">[144]</a></sup> Moreover, Hofter noted that the things that Broszat labeled <i>Resistenz</i> had no effect within the grander scheme of things on the ability of the Nazi regime to accomplish its objectives within Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_195_144-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_195-144">[144]</a></sup> Another of Broszat's critics, the German historian Klaus-Jürgen Müller argued that the term <i>Widerstand</i> should apply only to those having a "will to overcome the system" and that Broszat's <i>Resistenz</i> concept did too much to muddy the waters between by speaking of societal "immunity" to the regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_195_144-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_195-144">[144]</a></sup> A more sympathetic appraisal of the <i>Resistenz</i> concept came from the historians <a href="/wiki/Manfred_Messerschmidt" title="Manfred Messerschmidt">Manfred Messerschmidt</a> and Heinz Boberach who argued that <i>Widerstand</i> should be defined from the viewpoint of the Nazi state, and any activity that was contrary to the regime's wishes, such as listerning to <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> music, should be considered as a form of <i>Widerstand</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hans_Mommsen" title="Hans Mommsen">Hans Mommsen</a> wrote about the <i>Resistenz</i> concept that:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"This raises, of course, the issue of how to distinguish between resistance that intended to overthrow the system, and active <i>Resistenz</i> (though judged from the angle of convictions of the individual, this constitutes an artificial separation). Those who risked their lives to hide Jewish fellow citizens and acquire forged exit permits for them, those who tried to help Russian prisoners-of-war, those who, at their workplaces, fought for the rights of workers and refused to be indoctrinated by the German Labour Front, those who protested against the treatment of the Jewish population or publicly denounced the euthanasia programme, those who refused to obey criminal orders, those who as a powerless protest against Nazi war policies daubed slogans on walls at night-time, those who protected the persecuted and shared their ration cards with them-in a wider sense they all belonged to the resistance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Another viewpoint advanced in the debate was that of Mommsen, who cautioned against the use of overtly rigid terminology, and spoke of a wide type of "resistance practice" (<i>Widerstandspraxis</i>), by which he meant that there were different types and forms of resistance, and that resistance should be considered a "process", in which individuals came to increasingly reject the Nazi system in its entirety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_196_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_196-147">[147]</a></sup> As an example of resistance as a "process", Mommsen used the example of <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Goerdeler" title="Carl Friedrich Goerdeler">Carl Friedrich Goerdeler</a>, who initially supported the Nazis, became increasing disillusioned over Nazi economic policies while serving as Price Commissioner in the mid-1930s, and by the late 1930s was committed to Hitler's overthrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_196_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_196-147">[147]</a></sup> Mommsen described national-conservative resistance as "a resistance of servants of the state", who, over time, came to gradually abandon their former support of the regime, and instead steadily came to accept that the only way of bringing about fundamental change was to seek the regime’s destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> In regards to the idea of "resistance as a process", several historians have worked out typologies. The German historian <a href="/wiki/Detlev_Peukert" title="Detlev Peukert">Detlev Peukert</a> created a typology running from "nonconformity" (mostly done in private and not including total rejection of the Nazi system), "refusal of co-operation" (<i>Verweigerung</i>), "protest", and finally, "resistance" (those committed to the overthrow of the regime).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_205_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_205-149">[149]</a></sup> The Austrian historian Gerhard Botz argued for a typology starting with "deviant behavior" (minor acts of non-conformity), "social protest", and "political resistance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_205_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kershaw.2C_Ian_page_205-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>The British historian Sir <a href="/wiki/Ian_Kershaw" title="Ian Kershaw">Ian Kershaw</a> has argued that there are two approaches to the <i>Widerstand</i> question, one of which he calls the <i>fundamentalist</i> (dealing with those committed to overthrowing the Nazi regime) and the <i>societal</i> (dealing with forms of dissent in "everyday life").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> In Kershaw's viewpoint, the <i>Resistenz</i> concept works well in an <i><a href="/wiki/Alltagsgeschichte" title="Alltagsgeschichte">Alltagsgeschichte</a></i> approach, but works less well in the field of high politics, and moreover by focusing only on the "effect" of one's actions, fails to consider the crucial element of the "intention" behind one's actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Kershaw has argued that the term <i>Widerstand</i> should be used only for those working for the total overthrow of the Nazi system, and those engaging in behavior which was counter to the regime's wishes without seeking to overthrow the regime should be included under the terms opposition and dissent, depending upon their motives and actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Kershaw has used the <a href="/wiki/Edelweiss_Pirates" title="Edelweiss Pirates">Edelweiss Pirates</a> as an example of whose behavior initially fell under dissent, and who advanced from there to opposition and finally to resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Similarly, the American historian <a href="/wiki/Claudia_Koonz" title="Claudia Koonz">Claudia Koonz</a> in her 1992 article "Ethical Dilemmas and Nazi Eugenics”, argued that those who protested against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Action_T4" title="Action T4">Action T4</a> program, usually for religious reasons while remaining silent about the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocaust" title="Holocaust">Holocaust</a> cannot be considered as part of any resistance to the Nazis, and these protests can only be considered as a form of dissent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> In Kershaw's opinion, there were three bands ranging from dissent to opposition to resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> In Kershaw's view, there was much dissent and opposition within German society, but outside of the working-class, very little resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> Though Kershaw has argued that the <i>Resistenz</i> concept has much merit, overall he concluded that the Nazi regime had a broad basis of support and consensus, and it is correct to speak of "resistance without the people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>"You Did Not Bear The Shame, You Resisted, Sacrificing Your Life For Freedom, Justice and Honor."</p>
<p>-From the German Resistance Memorial, Berlin</p>

<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">History of the German Resistance, 1933-1945,Peter Hoffman, pg 38</span></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_resistance_to_Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl>
<dt>General</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Harold C. Deutsch "Symposium: New Perspectives on the German Resistance against National Socialism" pages 322–399 from <i>Central European History</i>, Volume 14, 1981.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Joachim_Fest" title="Joachim Fest">Fest, Joachim</a> <i>Plotting Hitler’s Death: The German Resistance to Hitler 1933–1945</i>, London: Weidenfeld &amp; Nicholson, 1996, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0297817744">ISBN 0-297-81774-4</a>.</li>
<li>Peter Hoffmann, <i>The History of the German Resistance 1933–1945</i>, McGill-Queen's University Press, 1996 <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0773515313">ISBN 0-7735-1531-3</a>.</li>
<li>Martyn Housden, <i>Resistance and Conformity in the Third Reich</i>, London ; New York : Routledge, 1997, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0415121337">ISBN 0-415-12133-7</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ian_Kershaw" title="Ian Kershaw">Ian Kershaw</a> <i>The Nazi Dictatorship Problems and Perspectives of Interpretation</i>, London: Arnold Press, 2000, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0340760281">ISBN 0-340-76028-1</a>
<ul>
<li><i>Hitler 1889–1936: Hubris</i> (W.W.Norton, 1998) and <i>Hitler 1936–1945: Nemesis</i> (W.W.Norton, 2000)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Klemens von Klemperer, <i>German Resistance Against Hitler:The Search for Allies Abroad 1938–1945</i>, Oxford : Clarendon Press, 1992, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0198219407">ISBN 0-19-821940-7</a></li>
<li>Richard Lamb, <i>The Ghosts of Peace, 1935–45</i> Michael Russell Publishing, 1987, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0859551407">ISBN 0-85955-140-7</a>.</li>
<li>David Clay Large (editor) <i>Contending with Hitler Varieties of German Resistance in the Third Reich</i>, Cambridge : Cambridge University Press, 1991, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0521466687">ISBN 0-521-46668-7</a>.</li>
<li>Annedore Leber, <i>The Conscience in Revolt : Portraits of the German Resistance 1933–1945</i> collected and edited by Annedore Leber in cooperation with <a href="/wiki/Willy_Brandt" title="Willy Brandt">Willy Brandt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Dietrich_Bracher" title="Karl Dietrich Bracher">Karl Dietrich Bracher</a>, Mainz : Hase &amp; Koehler, 1994 <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/3775813144">ISBN 3-7758-1314-4</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hans_Mommsen" title="Hans Mommsen">Hans Mommsen</a>, translated by Angus McGeoch <i>Alternatives to Hitler: German Resistance Under the Third Reich</i> Princeton : Princeton University Press, 2003, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0691116938">ISBN 0-691-11693-8</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Roger_Moorhouse" title="Roger Moorhouse">Roger Moorhouse</a>, <i>Killing Hitler : The Plots, The Assassins, And The Dictator Who Cheated Death</i> , New York : Bantam Books, 2006 <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0224071211">ISBN 0-224-07121-1</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hans_Rothfels" title="Hans Rothfels">Hans Rothfels</a> <i>The German Opposition to Hitler: An Assessment</i> Longwood Pr Ltd: London 1948, 1961, 1963, 1970 <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0854961194">ISBN 0-85496-119-4</a>.</li>
<li>Michael C. Thomsett <i>The German Opposition To Hitler : the Resistance, the Underground, and Assassination Plots, 1938–1945</i> Jefferson, N.C. ; London : McFarland, 1997, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0786403721">ISBN 0-7864-0372-1</a>.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>Themes</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Francis L. Carsten, <i>German Workers and the Nazis</i>, Aldershot, Hants, England : Scolar Press, 1995, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0859679985">ISBN 0-85967-998-5</a>.</li>
<li>Christoph Dippler "The German Resistance and the Jews" pages 51–93 from <i>Yad Vashem Studies</i>, Volume 16, 1984.</li>
<li>Peter Hoffmann "The German Resistance and the Holocaust" pages 105–126 from <i>Confront! Resistance in Nazi Germany</i> edited by John J. Michalczyk, New York: Peter Lang Publishers, 2004, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0820463175">ISBN 0-8204-6317-5</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ian_Kershaw" title="Ian Kershaw">Ian Kershaw</a> <i>Popular Opinion And Political Dissent In The Third Reich, Bavaria 1933–45</i>, Oxford : Clarendon Press, 1983, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0198219229">ISBN 0-19-821922-9</a></li>
<li>Tim Kirk, <i>Nazism and the Working Class in Austria: Industrial Unrest and Political Dissent in the National Community</i>, Cambridge ; New York : Cambridge University Press, 1996, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0521475015">ISBN 0-521-47501-5</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Claudia_Koonz" title="Claudia Koonz">Claudia Koonz</a> "Ethical Dilemmas and Nazi Eugenics: Single-Issue Dissent in Religious Contexts" pages S8–S31 from <i>Journal of Modern History</i>, Volume 64, 1992.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Roger_Manvell" title="Roger Manvell">Manvell, Roger</a> <i>The Canaris Conspiracy: The Secret Resistance to Hitler in the German Army</i>, New York: McKay, 1969,</li>
<li>Alan Merson <i>Communist Resistance in Nazi Germany</i>, London : Lawrence and Wishart, 1985, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0391033662">ISBN 0-391-03366-2</a>.</li>
<li>Klaus-Jürgen Müller “The German Military Opposition before the Second World War” pages 61–75 from <i>The Fascist Challenge and the Policy of Appeasement</i> edited by <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Mommsen" title="Wolfgang Mommsen">Wolfgang Mommsen</a> &amp; Lothar Kettenacker, George Allen &amp; Unwin: London, United Kingdom, 1983, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0049400681">ISBN 0-04-940068-1</a>.</li>
<li>Klaus-Jürgen Müller "The Structure and Nature of the National Conservative Opposition in Germany up to 1940" pages 133–178 from <i>Aspects of the Third Reich</i> edited by H.W. Koch, Macmillan: London, United Kingdom, 1985 <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0333352726">ISBN 0-333-35272-6</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Timothy_Mason" title="Timothy Mason">Timothy Mason</a> "The Workers' Opposition in Nazi Germany" pages 120–137 from <i>History Workshop Journal</i>, Volume 11, 1981.</li>
<li>Jeremy Noakes "The Oldenburg Crucifix Struggle of November 1936 A Case Study in Opposition in the Third Reich" pages 210–233 from <i>The Shaping of the Nazi State</i> edited by Peter Stachura, London : Croom Helm ; New York : Barnes &amp; Noble, 1978, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0856644714">ISBN 0-85664-471-4</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Detlev_Peukert" title="Detlev Peukert">Detlev Peukert</a> <i>Inside Nazi Germany : Conformity, Opposition and Racism in Everyday Life</i> London : Batsford, 1987 <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/071345217X">ISBN 0-7134-5217-X</a>.</li>
</ul>
